FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Zurich's eco-warrior leads quiet green revolution; Interview

By Chris Flood 
1,125 words
16 June 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
04
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Chief investment officer Cecilia Reyes tells Chris Flood 'doing well and doing good are not mutually exclusive'

Cecilia Reyes would not strike many people as a typical "eco-warrior".

But the immaculately dressed chief investment officer of Zurich Insurance Group is a quiet revolutionary.

Petite and softly spoken, Ms Reyes was a driving force behind Zurich's decision last year to invest $1bn in "green" bonds that finance projects aimed at mitigating climate change.

"Doing well financially and doing good are not mutually exclusive. We can do both at the same time," says the 55-year old, a point she emphasises several times during the interview.

But sceptics question whether institutional investors such as Zurich will commit sufficient capital to make a significant difference in the fight against global warming. The $1bn investment is less than 0.5 per cent of the insurer's $214bn portfolio.

Ms Reyes counters that the $1bn commitment is "just a starting point" designed to attract other issuers and new investors to the small but rapidly growing green bond market .

"Why would you not invest in green bonds if they provide returns commensurate with their investment risks and at same time support environmentally friendly projects?" asks Ms Reyes, who was born in Manila and now has dual Swiss and Philippine nationality.

She argues that there is "huge potential" for green bonds, even if these instruments grow to just 1 per cent of the $57tn asset pool held by institutional investors globally.

Green bonds, says Ms Reyes, are one of the pillars of Zurich's responsible investing strategy.

"Responsible investing is part of our ambition to be the best global insurer. But we cannot achieve this without embedding responsible investing standards throughout both our core business activities, insurance and investment."

Her aim is to make responsible investing "part of Zurich's DNA".

While integrating environmental, social and governance (ESG) considerations into an investment process might sound straightforward, evaluating how these factors affect risk and returns of individual securities and different asset classes is not easy, she says.

Zurich uses external managers to run about two-thirds of its investment assets. Ms Reyes acknowledges that Zurich's internal and external managers need to do more work to properly assess the importance of non-financial factors such as environmental risks inherent in companies' business models.

"If non-financial risks are integrated in the assessment of investment opportunities, we truly believe that we will deliver better risk-adjusted returns over the long term," she says.

But sceptics doubt if ESG policies really work. A recent analysis by Hermes Fund Managers found environmental and social policies were a complete waste of time in terms of investment returns.

Ms Reyes, however, is convinced that ESG policies can drive change.

"As it becomes clear that investors ascribe value to non-financial ESG factors, companies will act accordingly," she says.

As part of its responsible investing agenda, Zurich is reshaping its private equity portfolio. It is raising allocations to PE managers that have declared an interest in making "impact" investments to address pressing social or environmental issues as well as generating financial returns.

Property and cleantech funds that invest in technologies such as solar and hydro power are other areas of interest for Zurich's strategy of bringing impact investing into the mainstream.

It is also working with a non-governmental organisation to deliver health services in the developing world, an area where Miss Reyes sees potential for further expansion.

"We believe that return-seeking private capital has an important role to play in helping NGOs deliver a positive impact in the communities [where] they are working," says Ms Reyes.

She points out that many observers confuse being a responsible investor with ethical investing. But assets such as tobacco and fossil fuels eschewed by those with an ethical mandate are not excluded from Zurich's portfolio.

Ms Reyes believes excluding large parts of the investable universe would not be consistent with Zurich's fiduciary duties or its aim of generating superior risk-adjusted returns.

However, some analysts argue that Zurich's investment performance has weakened, requiring holdings of high coupon bonds to be sold last year to support dividends.

It is a charge Ms Reyes rejects. She says Zurich has outperformed its internal liability benchmark by 800 basis points over the past decade and that investment returns have improved in the five years since the end of 2008 compared with the previous five-year period.

She acknowledges that Zurich has "benefited hugely" from the long bull market in fixed income and insists that the company has little to fear from rising interest rates, which will increase the discount applied to future liabilities.

She also explains that while conventional fund managers seek to maximise risk-adjusted returns, the investment strategy of an insurer is designed to ensure it can meet all its liabilities (future claims).

With operations in more than 30 countries, investment management for Zurich is a "highly complex" business. Zurich's assets are not run as a single pool but managed in each country where it operates.

As Zurich believes markets are "fairly efficient over time", Ms Reyes says it aims to be "very conscious of the risks we take, those which are rewarded and those which are not".

A key principle is to avoid "unrewarded risks" such as betting on the future path of interest rates. It also avoids concentrated portfolios, frequent trading and investing in complex or opaque instruments, such as highly leveraged structured credit products.

She admits that there is still more to do for Zurich as a thought leader, championing the importance of being a responsible investor with industry peers, fund managers and asset owners.

Achieving this ambitious agenda should win favour with investors and eco-warriors alike.

Curriculum vitae

Cecilia Reyes

Born 1959

Education

1982 BSc in management/ industrial engineering, Ateneo de Manila University, Manila

1984 MBA (finance), University of Hawaii

1992 PhD (finance), London Business School

Career

1990-95 Credit Suisse in Zurich in various roles in asset management, global treasury and securities trading

1995 Financial engineer and risk consultant, ING Barings in London

1997 Director and head of trading risk analytics, ING Barings

2000 Head of risk analytics, ING Asset Management in Amsterdam

2001 Zurich Insurance, regional manager for group investments with responsibility for North America

2004 Regional manager for Europe and international businesses

2006 Head of investment strategy implementation

Since 2010 Chief investment officer

Zurich Insurance Group

Assets $424.5bn (end March 2014)

Number of employees 55,000

Headquarters Zurich, with large offices in London, Chicago, Hong Kong. Operations in more than 30 countries with clients in 170 countries

Listing SIX Swiss Exchange

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Half of fund groups to disappear

By Madison Marriage 
294 words
16 June 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
02
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Half of fund companies will disappear by 2030 as the asset management industry struggles to keep up with technological change, KPMG has warned.

Mid-sized fund groups with 100bn-300bn in assets are most at risk of dying out, says Tom Brown, global head of investment management at the consultancy.

Mr Brown believes many fund companies in this bracket have not recognised the importance of data analytics to drive investment performance or understand their client base.

He said: "If you don't get either of those trends, in 15 years' time you won't exist in your current form.

"This is a bit of a wake-up call to the industry. Unless the current players really [accept] the fact that it is a different world they are facing, over time they will be marginalised and taken over, or just shrink."

Fund companies that are slow to adapt could find themselves replaced or bought out by big technology groups such as Apple or Google, or large retailers that could become "the next big powerhouses in investment management", added Ian Smith, financial services strategy partner at KPMG.

He said: "Trusted brands that resonate and appeal to a more diverse client base as well as the younger generation, may be able to build scale quickly."

Joerg Ambrosius, head of asset manager solutions in Europe at State Street, the financial services company, said it was "definitely right" that many names will disappear from the fund industry as midsized asset managers shrink or merge.

Recent research carried out for State Street showed that 72 per cent of senior asset management executives believe there will be an increased opportunity for acquisitions in the next 12 months alone.

The Family Strategy for Building an Empire

By Keiko Morris 
897 words
16 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Anthony Malkin's family has been a player in New York City real estate for four generations.

So it may come as no surprise that Mr. Malkin, chief executive of Empire State Realty Trust Inc., regards his familiarity with the city's building-owning families as an asset for one growth strategy he has in mind.

In an increasingly pricey Manhattan market, he believes Empire can trade company stakes for properties owned by families, allowing the sellers to defer the tax consequences.

Other publicly traded real-estate investment trusts have used the strategy, but he said it made particular sense in New York City, where a significant amount of the commercial property remains in family hands.

"I believe these are better opportunities than going out into the market and just competing against the flood of capital," he said.

It has been eight months since Empire State Realty went public, and its stock has steadily risen in value after an initial stumble. The company made news in May when it announced it would exercise its options on ground and operating leases on a pair of Garment District buildings.

Mr. Malkin's immediate priority is the continuing overhaul of the company's Manhattan properties, especially the Empire State Building, and the effort to lease its commercial space at higher market-rate rents. But analysts see the logic in his inclination to offer equity stakes in the company to acquire assets.

"Tony Malkin likely has an impressive Rolodex to call from, although the jury is still out on how receptive an audience he'll find," said Jed Reagan, an analyst at Green Street Advisors Inc. "His chances will likely improve if Manhattan property values continue to rise and Empire can further its track record in the public market."

Sales prices of Manhattan office buildings rose 55% from the first quarter of 2012 through the same period this year, according to real estate data firm Real Capital Analytics Inc.

Empire State Realty's stock began trading in October at about $13 a share. Since then, the stock's value has risen 27%. Major institutional investors such as Invesco Ltd. and Vanguard Group have bought shares, Mr. Malkin noted.

The company has made headway renovating and modernizing its buildings, filling the available space with larger tenants and raising rents, analysts said. Many of the leases that have been in place for a while are below market rates, they noted. It has been replacing smaller tenants with tenants such as LinkedIn Corp. and Shutterstock Inc. Both signed leases for space in the Empire State Building.

So far, the company has spent about $450 million on redeveloping its Manhattan office portfolio. It expects to spend another $100 million to $140 million though 2016, almost all of it at the Empire State Building.

At the end of the first quarter, the company's buildings, many of which sit near the coveted Midtown South area, were about 87% occupied, up 7.2 percentage points from a year earlier, company executives said during a conference call. That 13% vacancy rate compares with 7.9% for Midtown South and 11.1% for Midtown, according to Cushman & Wakefield Inc.

Empire State Realty announced in May that it would exercise its options to acquire the ground and operating leases at 112 W. 34th St. and 1400 Broadway for $734 million cash, common stock and operating partnership units. The company has been managing the properties for nearly a decade.

The building on West 34th Street, which is across from Macy's Herald Square store, has about 90,000 square feet of retail that will become available in 2016 and could bring much higher rents, analysts said.

"We have been intimately familiar with these assets," said Mr. Malkin, who, with his father, Peter Malkin, recused himself from a board vote on the deal. "We weren't taking a risk."

The two buildings will help the company diversify its sources of income and the value of its portfolio. That value continues to rest heavily on the broad shoulders of the Empire State Building and its income-generating observatory, said Jordan Sadler, a senior analyst at Keybanc Capital Markets Inc.

Last year, the observatory generated $101.8 million in revenue and drew a record 4.3 million visitors, the company said.

"While it's a great business and a great opportunity, it just represents a concentration that could be more exposed to [factors such as] annual tourism as opposed to the overall economic conditions of New York City," Mr. Sadler said.

Down the road, Mr. Malkin said, he aimed to maintain the company's balance sheet with its modest debt ratio.

At the end of the first quarter, the company said its debt-to-market capitalization ratio was about 24%.

Green Street Advisors, which uses its own estimation of the company's market value, puts Empire's debt ratio at 28%, including expected debt that will be assumed with the two new properties.

This compares to an average debt ratio of 41% for the U.S. real-estate investment trust office sector. More important, Mr. Malkin said, a strong balance sheet with low debt would allow the company to "deploy capital when others cannot."

"We are always going to be omnivorous opportunivores," Mr. Malkin said.

"Gus" Sauter, a senior consultant to and former chief investment officer of Vanguard Group Inc.; Eleanor Blayney, consumer advocate of the Certified Financial Planner Board of Standards; Charles Rotblut, a vice president with the American Association of Individual Investors; and Matt Hougan, president of ETF analytics and global head of editorial for IndexUniverse LLC.

The Family Strategy for Building an Empire; Anthony Malkin Regards Familiarity With Real Estate Families as Asset for Growth Strategy

Global Finance: Fed Wants Consequences If Banks' Plans Go Awry

By Alan Zibel 
479 words
13 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

WASHINGTON -- The Federal Reserve wants to keep banks from buying back shares and raising dividends if they don't fulfill their stated goals to shore up financing.

The Fed, in a proposal released Thursday, said it would limit banks' ability to distribute dividends or buy back shares if firms don't raise the levels of capital proposed as part of their official capital plan submitted to the central bank.

The Fed said some banks, as part of its annual stress tests, "included issuances of capital instruments in their capital plans, but didn't execute these planned issuances." The Fed said such behavior has the "potential to undermine" its ability to evaluate the financial health of banks, a goal of its annual test of lenders' financial strength.

The Fed didn't indicate which banks have failed to follow through on their planned capital raises.

Under the central bank's proposal, if a bank pledged to raise $50 million in common stock but only raised $25 million, the bank would be required to reduce its dividends and share repurchases by $25 million.

The Fed's stress-test results, released annually, have become a yearly ritual with major implications for the banking system. This year, the Fed rejected Citigroup Inc.'s capital plan, and Bank of America Corp. was forced to scale back a plan to buy back shares and boost its dividend after finding an error in its capital level.

The central bank also proposed more-technical changes, including telling banks that they aren't required to submit revised versions of their capital plans if their initial proposals are rejected by the Fed. Banks can choose to resubmit if they want to seek board approval in the wake of a rejection.

Anna Krayn, a director in the stress-testing practice at Moody'sAnalytics, said the Fed proposal "could tie managements' hands in ways that might not be very beneficial" to the bank. In the past, she said, a bank might have planned to raise new capital but have delayed doing so because of market conditions. That might be harder to do under the Fed proposal, because it could prompt the regulator to restrict dividends in response, she said.

The Fed also proposed to change the date at which banks are required to submit their stress-test plans to April 5 from Jan. 5.

Bankers have complained privately that the current schedule requires them to work heavy hours through the holidays in late December to meet the January deadline.

Wells Fargo & Co. Chief Executive John Stumpf hinted at some of those frustrations at a conference last December, saying the process "means holidays for us" that include "an hour or two off for Thanksgiving."

---

Christina Rexrode contributed to this article.

### picture data found, picture dimensions are 254 by 40, depth 1
pict039.png

Special
The One Change That Could Make HR Departments More Effective

2,075 words
12 June 2014
18:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Most employees have at least one complaint about HR departments. With this in mind, we asked The Experts: What one change could make HR departments more effective?

This discussion relates to a recent Leadership Report and formed the basis of a discussion on The Experts blog in April 2014.

What HR Departments Can Learn From Hollywood

ROSABETH MOSS KANTER: HR should get into show business. If talent is key to company success, then HR professionals should take cues from Broadway, Hollywood and Bollywood. After all, HR people are casting managers, looking for the best people and best fit, in order to give audiences the best experience a core Disney premise. HR departments can be set and costume designers, creating a great work environment that's disciplined but also fun. They are production directors, making sure that everyone works together well. They reward star performers. They should redefine career paths to be more like the Hollywood model, with fewer fixed jobs and more temporary teams. They increasingly live in flexible project-based organizations, in which teams are brought together to create a "show" and then disband and re-form for another one.

HR departments have always been a little schizophrenic. There's the side that is part of finance and administration counting heads, managing the payroll, processing entries and exits. And there's another part that should be allied with marketing and sales selling the company to prospective and actual employees, and through culture-building or organization development, ensuring that employees help sell its virtues to customers. I think computers can handle the routine tasks on the administration side. I'd like to see the marketing side of HR get more emphasis. HR should be at the center of developing community-service projects that motivate employees while they improve the world. And they should give line managers the tools and training to put on the best possible shows for customers.

Rosabeth Moss Kanter (@RosabethKanter) holds the Ernest L. Arbuckle professorship at Harvard Business School, where she specializes in strategy, innovation and leadership for change.

How HR Departments Can Create an Innovation Culture

MICHAEL WOLF: The mission and focus of HR departments needs to reflect that innovation happens in teams, not departments or divisions. HR must shift from carrying out personnel tasks such as benefits and policies to playing an active role in forming, nurturing and rewarding great teams bringing together the human resources to innovate through new products, new features and new ways of delighting customers.

Practically every CEO talks about building an "innovation culture." HR should be central to cultivating that culture. The reality is that companies need to create examples of innovation successes to give the rest of their organization a proof of concept that they can emulate.

HR's role can be critical in casting the right set of talents to form those teams. It isn't enough for HR to recruit people through the outside hiring process. They must look inside of their organizations and recognize individuals who strike the right balance between a diverse perspective and the ability to collaborate. HR can also advise senior executives to select effective group leaders. The weight of bringing together the right mix of people is even greater since small groups are usually the most effective. In fact, the best teams should be sized by the "two pizza rule" (a term alternatively attributed to either Jeff Bezos of Amazon or Brad Smith of Intuit) no larger than could be fed by two pizzas. Finally, HR can act as the strategic business partner to set out the team's objectives and ensure that the team is on track to achieve them.

Ultimately, great teams depend on great people, and teams can change a corporate culture. By moving from a centralized service function into an active role of facilitating small groups of the best people, HR can become the valued enabler and advocate of innovation.

Michael J. Wolf is founder and managing director of technology and strategy consulting company Activate Inc. He formerly served on theYahoo board of directors and as president and chief operating officer of MTV Networks.

What Most HR Executives Are Lacking

RITA GUNTHER MCGRATH: Companies should insist that nobody gets to have an important job in HR unless they have actually run a P&L, developed a strategy or executed against a major organizational change. The big problem with many HR departments is that they are staffed by career HR people who may be very capable but whose knowledge of actual leadership activities is purely theoretical. The best HR people I've worked with don't just echo the eternal HR whine "We want to be business partners and have a seat at the table." The best HR people know how to see opportunities for improving the organization, know how to tailor incentive and development plans to foster the engagement and retention of critical people, and don't wait to be invited to the table, they earn their spot there.

To give two examples: One European client came to Columbia to design an executive leadership program for people who had achieved positions of importance primarily because of technical expertise. The main driver of the program housed, temporarily, in the HR department had found resources for the needs assessment, benchmarked best practices, persuaded senior leadership that this was really important, and played a pivotal role in what became a highly regarded three-year intervention whose effects have been lasting.

In a second example, for a major conglomerate making an acquisition, it was the HR team that went through the talent of the target company, identified the "must keeps" and put together a pitch for the CEO to make a personal phone call outlining the post-acquisition role for the desirable person, vastly increasing the chance that they would remain with the merged company. Now that's driving effectiveness and truly strategic outcomes.

Oh, and one more thing. Having respected line managers serve a stint in HR would do a lot for its reputation as a career backwater.

Rita Gunther McGrath ( @rgmcgrath) is an associate professor at Columbia Business School and author of the recent book"The End of Competitive Advantage: How to Keep Your Strategy Moving as Fast as Your Business."

How 'Hackathons' Can Help Companies Find the Best Employees

KARL ULRICH: The "hackathon" has emerged as an effective way to find exceptional engineering talent. Aspiring software developers gather for a two-day event in which individuals and teams compete to develop the best application in response to some compelling challenge. Judges (and increasingly sponsors) observe the results and designate the most successful efforts. For instance, the PennApps hackathon at my own university attracts more than 2,000 applicants from all over the country and is sponsored by Facebook, Amazon and Google, among others.

For software engineering, a domain in which a great coder can be worth 10 times more than an average coder, the hackathon is a powerful tool to accurately reveal who is great and who is only average. Plus, the mere fact that you travel to a hackathon, and commit 48 hours without sleep to the quest, reveals quite a bit about your abilities. No wonder the most powerful technology companies line up to sponsor the events and observe the outcomes.

But, in management more generally, say in operations or marketing, isn't a great employee worth 10 times more than an average employee? Might there be an event analogous to the hackathon for identifying and recruiting managerial talent? True, it's hard to imagine the appeal of a competition for who can run the best meeting or make the best PowerPoint deck. But, imagine a series of events over two days for revealing who is great at, say, generating ideas, giving a sales pitch, organizing a team to accomplish a goal, or improving the productivity of a process. We know that conventional interviews are terrible at predicting job performance.

Let's take inspiration from the hackathon and devise some fun, compelling, and challenging events that reveal those who are great at what they do, not just those who went to the right schools and can ace an interview.

Karl Ulrich is vice dean of innovation and CIBC Professor of Entrepreneurship and E-Commerce at the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School.

HR Needs to Get on the Front Lines

WAYNE MEKJIAN: HR departments work hard to help us recruit top talent in our respective industries. As we look at what lies ahead of us in the technology world, I encourage our HR colleagues to get closer to our business and immerse themselves into what we do every day. What obstacles do we face? What new or unique skill sets have we developed?

From there, we can have better discussions about training and recruitment needs to ensure we stay competitive in the marketplace. We may discover that in some locations, like New York, we need to have a completely different recruiting strategy than we do in Charlotte, N.C.

Wayne Mekjian is executive vice president and head of information services at Wells Fargo.

Why HR Departments Need More Authority

GIANPIERO PETRIGLIERI: For all the focus organizations put on change and innovation, human-resource departments are often among their most conservative quarters.

This is evident in the long-standing popularity of "best practices" that help replicate traditional power structures, and of "competency models" that play down the complex and context-dependent nature of managing and leading, and being recognized as doing so.

It is even more evident in HR's current romance with "big data" and its promise to predict performance, or more precisely, to select and reward people most like those who have been credited with the company's success in the past.

There is nothing wrong with processes, models and data, of course. The problem is turning them into a fetish with the pretense that they remove human subjectivity and bias, when in reality they just endorse the subjectivity and consolidate the biases of those people and practices cast as models for everyone else.

Human resources' conservatism, in short, resides in cloaking leaders' choices under a veil of pseudoscientific inevitability that inhibits the company from questioning whether reliance on those leaders, those choices and on the past is desirable or wise.

The inability to question leaders, choices and the past, in turn, makes it difficult to imagine a different future let alone build one. No wonder innovation is such an elusive aspiration.

HR's conservatism is hardly due to the timidity or ideology of individuals who occupy the function. Quite the contrary, HR executives I meet are often frustrated by the constraints it poses on their desire and ability to initiate or support strategic change.

It is most often a product of the ambiguous status the HR function still holds in many organizations.

While corporate rhetoric celebrates it as a strategic instrument, HR is often treated as a maintenance tool. The aim to stay aligned to the business so as to create value often translates into the demand to keep the organization running smoothly to earn legitimacy.

Take leadership development, for example, arguably the most forward-looking among HR's duties. Most of the practices that go under that banner have less to do with helping employees make their mark on the company's future, and more to do with socializing them into the company's current culture and giving them knowledge and skills to perform in their current job.

Having to earn their legitimacy by keeping the business running leaves many HR departments with no real mandate and few resources to attempt or support experimentation the very work they would happily do more of and companies need.

What changes, then, would make that possible?

Structurally, human resources needs to have a seat on the executive committee, so as to question and help shape strategy rather than simply having to bolster it.

Culturally, the ubiquitous corporate rhetoric of alignment needs to give way to one that celebrates committed divergence.

Those changes would free HR departments up to do more than keeping things running smoothly. They would empower HR to help the company question and reimagine, not just hold on to and replicate, itself.

Gianpiero Petriglieri(@gpetriglieri) is associate professor of organizational behavior at Insead, where he directs themanagement acceleration programfor emerging leaders.

Ready or Not, Here Come the Asian Regulators

By William Mcgovern 
896 words
12 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
13
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Global investigations of banking activity are becoming a fact of life given that banking is now a global activity. So far most of the headlines have come from Western regulatory inquiries into the activities of Western banks in Western markets. But more of this heat is destined to shift to Asia, and bankers, local regulators and Western authorities alike will face new challenges.

Banks have come under international scrutiny most recently for alleged activities in foreign-exchange markets, where regulators charge that some banks manipulated benchmark rates. In many ways, this is a typical example of such an investigation. It involves 12 regulators on four continents investigating 15 banks, but with U.S. authorities firmly taking the lead.

Already there are signs that greater regulatory scrutiny will arise in Asia, too. The Hong Kong Monetary Authority, for example, made clear its intention to investigate potentially manipulative foreign-exchange trading when it ordered several Hong Kong banks to engage in an internal review and share the results with HKMA investigators.

In other instances, Asian regulators are starting to run ahead of Western authorities. For example, China's regulators aggressively investigated British pharmaceutical company GlaxoSmithKline, showing that they are willing to develop their own leads and break new investigative ground where U.S. or British regulators may be stymied by various logistical hurdles.

Asian regulators increasingly have a stake in investigations given the volume of financial activity occurring in the region. They also have a political motive -- which is not unreasonable -- to show their citizens that they are cracking down on illegal activity in their jurisdictions. More worrying, money may play a role, too. The still-unfolding investigation into allegations of rigging the London Interbank Offer Rate, or LIBOR, has yielded $6 billion in fines for the U.S. and British governments. Regulators in emerging markets may follow the U.S. example of aggressively defining fraud as a path to reap massive windfalls for their treasuries.

One challenge this poses for banks is the difficulty of crafting effective compliance rules and conducting internal investigations across a large number of geographic areas. Already this is on display in cases such as the forex investigation, in which bank directors and senior managers are scrambling to quantify their exposure. Management will be under increasing pressure to improve internal processes, take disciplinary action where warranted and manage the torrent of subpoenas and other demands from regulators.

Western banks also will need to adjust to the realities of being investigated by new jurisdictions. Each regulator brings with it different tools, procedural considerations and timelines. Defense counsel representing banking institutions and their managers must take note. For instance, Asian regulators and prosecutors are often able to compel individuals to answer questions. Where those regulators take the lead, U.S.-based defense counsel, conditioned to Constitutional protections, may struggle to navigate this more nuanced terrain.

Similarly, data privacy and state secrecy restrictions abound in Asia and result in much tighter controls on information flow. Where U.S. bank lawyers are accustomed to open access to documents to formulate a defense to charges, when faced with a purely local investigation run by local regulators they may encounter difficulties in accessing information stored overseas. Lawyers and defense counsel may find that they have to head overseas simply to review documents and formulate a defense.

Asian regulators also will face changes as they seek to take the lead on cross-border investigations. Especially as Asia-originated financial flows increase and regional watchdogs grow ever more experienced, they will be less willing to wait to follow the lead of other regulators. Authorities are starting to engage in their own risk-based examinations of banks and flexing their enforcement muscles more. It is only a matter of time before we witness a high-profile investigation of an Asia-based bank, led by a local regulator.

In taking the lead on global investigations local Asian regulators will likely need to adopt hiring and investigative practices that mirror their Western counterparts. The capability to conduct large scale document review using artificial intelligence tools is essential. China recently announced use of high-tech algorithms to detect insider trading, showing that they are adopting Western style investigation tools. Similarly, local regulators will need to hire lawyers and investigative staff who can pursue creative approaches rather than cookie-cutter approaches which are doomed to fail in today's hyper-complex financial markets.

American regulators will need to adjust to this new world, too. In recent years, American and European authorities have developed an informal framework for their cooperation on such matters, including exchange of documents and assistance from local authorities on extradition of individuals. That model appears to be working increasingly well as the number of cross border requests for assistance climb each year. Cooperating with Asian counterparts who have taken the initiative in launching an investigation will be a new experience for U.S. officials who are used to setting the investigative agenda and dictating the terms of cooperation.

While the emergence of Asian regulators in the global investigation scene will take time to fully develop, the process is well underway. Managers and Western regulators can't afford to ignore this trend.

---

Mr. McGovern is a Hong Kong-based partner at Kobre & Kim LLP.

The Property Report: Deutsche Unit Pays Up for a Piece of Florida Mall

By Robbie Whelan 
927 words
11 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
24
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The real-estate asset-management arm of Deutsche Bank AG has purchased a 50% stake in a Florida mall at one of the highest prices paid for a mall since the downturn.

Deutsche Asset & Wealth Management paid $375 million for half of St. Johns Town Center, a large open-air shopping mall in Jacksonville whose tenants include Louis Vuitton, The Gap and an Apple Store. The deal values the 621,000 square-foot mall at $750 million, not including some $270 million in debt on the property, according to several people familiar with the transaction.

The deal comes as prices are rising overall for top-quality malls with strong sales numbers, big sales markets and low vacancy rates. Low interest rates on bonds and a scarcity of high-quality properties for sale has driven up prices of assets like St. Johns Town Center, which has 176 stores and serves a large market stretching from Southern Georgia to Central Florida.

"For institutional property, the buyers are stacked up 10 or 20 deep," says Dan Fasulo, managing director of Real Capital Analytics Inc., a research firm. "There's a lack of investment alternatives, and it just keeps driving capital into commercial real estate."

The high price of the deal is reflected in how much Deutsche initially will get in yield -- or the capitalization rate, as it is known in the real-estate industry.

Cap rates, which measure the ratio of a property's annual net income to its value, are a common way of evaluating commercial-real-estate transactions. Falling cap rates typically are a reflection of rising values.

In the case of the St. Johns deal, the cap rate is about 4%, which is thought to be one of the lowest cap rates for the sale of a mall since the real-estate downturn. This year, the average cap rate on mall sales has been 5.8%, down from 6.8% last year, according to Real Capital Analytics.

"It's a really aggressive price," says Real Capital's Mr. Fasulo of the St. Johns deal. "But as much as it feels aggressive to me, you have pools of capital running around the world looking for a premium to government bonds."

The seller of the 50% stake is Ben Carter, the Atlanta-based real-estate developer who built St. Johns Town Center in 2005. The other one-half stake in the mall is owned by Simon Property Group Inc., the world's largest mall owner. Simon is retaining its stake and will continue to manage the property.

Mr. Carter, who also has property investments in Atlanta and Savannah, Ga., said in an interview he sold his stake in the Jacksonville mall because he wants to focus on different projects. He also said he thought he could get a good price because there are few high-quality, superregional malls on the market.

"My new business strategy is outlet malls and urban development. I don't see a great need in the Southeast for regional malls or large outlet centers," Mr. Carter said. "They're not making these assets anymore. There are very few of them in private hands, and the premium assets don't really trade."

St. Johns Town Center had an occupancy rate of 99% and sales of between $650 and $700 a square foot last year, according to Mr. Carter and brokers who worked on the deal.

Target Corp. and Dillard's Inc. own their own stores at the shopping center, and in October, upscale clothing retailer Nordstrom is expected to open a 138,000-square-foot location in the mall. About 70% of retailers at the mall are due for a lease renewal in the next year, Mr. Carter said. That will give the new owners a chance to renegotiate leases and increase rents.

Deutsche Asset & Wealth Management, formerly known as RREEF, invests in publicly traded real-estate investment trusts, private equity and debt, in addition to making direct property investments across the globe.

In recent months, the firm has bought office buildings in Fort Lauderdale, Fla., stakes in French department-store companies, apartments in suburban Chicago and a hotel in Times Square.

In a 2014 strategic outlook report produced by Deutsche in February, the company observed that "the split between the best- and worst-performing shopping centers grows ever wider and seems to be enduring," and said that it prefers to invest in "dominant regional malls and unique retail offerings" such as urban "high street" retail.

Green Street Advisors, a Newport Beach, Calif.-based real-estate research firm, estimates that last year, the highest-quality malls in the country, dubbed "A++" malls, produced $945 in sales a square foot of space. D-malls, the lowest-quality grade, produced $140 a square foot last year. Based on its sales per square foot last year, St. Johns Town Center would fall somewhere between an A and A+-rated mall.

The sale to Deutsche is "a good sign for owners who have this quality of properties in their portfolios," said Daniel Busch, an analyst who covers retail real estate for Green Street. "All the focus recently has been on selling low quality properties, the B malls. That's what's been on the market. These high-end transactions are few and far between. This transaction gives us a lot of confidence that values are holding up at the high end."

Stocks stall as bulls pause for breath

By Jamie Chisholm, Global Markets Commentator 
877 words
10 June 2014
15:55
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Tuesday 15:55 BST. Bourses are mixed as bulls pause for breath after a rally that left global stocks at record levels.

The more tentative tone is boosting the "haven" yen and leaving industrial metals weaker. The dollar index is up 0.2 per cent at 80.81, challenging four-month highs, while gold is up $9 to $1,261 an ounce.

Wall Street's S&P 500 is down 0.1 per cent from Monday's record closing high of 1,951.3. The FTSE Asia-Pacific index climbed 0.3 per cent to its highest close in six years and the FTSE Eurofirst 300 is inching up 0.2 per cent to its best level since January 2008, even as weakness in resources groups sees London's FTSE 100 retreat 0.3 per cent.

All this leaves the FTSE All-World index easing almost 0.1 per cent from its all-time high of 281.3, having been driven up of late by hopes global monetary policy will remain ultra-accommodative for some time to come despite evidence of improving economic conditions in the US and China.

There is little in the way of fresh news to challenge that rationale, so the timid mood in the current session is being blamed on a bout of caution as some argue that investors are overly sanguine and many developed markets have come too far, too fast.

Historically low volatility gauges suggest a reluctance to hedge amid a belief central bank policy will support asset prices. Meanwhile, the 14-day relative strength index for the S&P 500, a measure of the Wall Street benchmark's momentum, is 71.9, where any figure above 70 is considered in "overbought" territory.

Strategas Research notes that the proportion of bullish US investors "is now in the 95th percentile, which historically has produced below average forward returns".

Investors' attraction to equities has been fed partly by the meagre returns on offer from fixed income products as central banks intervene to suppress yields and inflation is considered quiescent.

Though still near historic lows, in the current session the yields on 10-year US and German paper are up 3 basis points to 2.64 per cent and 1.41 per cent respectively.

"Peripheral" eurozone bond yields are hardening by several basis points but remain near record lows following the European Central Bank's allusion to possible quantitative easing to tackle the bloc's deflationary pressures. The possibility of ECB QE is pushing the euro down 0.4 per cent to $1.3540, close to four-month lows.

More downward pressure on global prices could come from China, where producer prices fell for the 27th consecutive month, down 1.4 per cent in May from a year earlier, versus a decline of 2.0 per cent in April.

The data reflect weak demand as a result of "rampant overcapacity in sectors including steel and cement, which is keeping down prices of raw materials", said Moody'sAnalytics.

China's annual consumer price inflation was 2.5 per cent in May, higher than anticipated and up from an 18-month low of 1.8 per cent in April.

"Today's inflation data confirm our view that broader price pressures are stable and that concerns about [CPI] deflation, sparked by a shortlived fall in headline inflation in April, were overplayed," wrote Julian Evans-Pritchard at Capital Economics.

Hopes that the benign CPI would allow the People's Bank of China more leeway in loosening policy bolstered China's stock market, pushing the Shanghai Composite up 1.1 per cent and helping Hong Kong's Hang Seng gain 0.9 per cent.

Indeed, the PBoC is already tweaking financial regulations to encourage more lending, reducing the amount of cash reserves some smaller institutions must hold at the central bank.

Despite the market's gains, some analysts said the move was unlikely to have much of an impact because it targeted only certain banks.

"The PBoC has been resisting calls for blanket RRR [required reserve ratio] cuts as they believe the banking system is not short of liquidity and they do not want to further add leverage to the economy," wrote Jian Chang at Barclays.

Industrial commodities, usually highly sensitive to perceptions of China demand, appear to be focusing on the message sent by falling manufacturing prices. Copper is down 0.1 per cent at $6,667 a tonne, mirroring declines for most other metals. Brent crude is 23 cents lower at $1109.76 a barrel as traders keep a wary eye on violent militant activity in Iraq.

In Japan, the Nikkei 225 started with solid gains, building on Monday's three-month high, but turned as the yen strengthened, weighing on shares of exporters. The dollar is now down 0.2 per cent to Y102.37.

New data were discouraging: Japan's tertiary industry index, a key measure of output from the service sector, showed a 5.4 per cent decline in April, its worst monthly reading since the March 2011 earthquake and tsunami. The Nikkei 225 closed down 0.9 per cent.

Additional reporting by Patrick McGee in Hong Kong

Index Ventures Raises $543 Million for New Fund; Venture Capital Firm Aims to Seed Early-Stage Startups

Amir Mizroch

LONDON Venture-capital firm Index Ventures said it raised  ¬400 million ($543 million) for a new fund aimed at seeding early-stage startups the latest foray by one of Europe's most active tech investors.

The company, with offices in Geneva, London and San Francisco, scours for investments in the U.S. and Europe. It has traditionally focused on nascent startup hubs like Tel Aviv, London, Berlin, Paris, Stockholm and the Nordics.

The firm is coming off an especially busy 12 months: Seven companies it sunk money into either went through initial public offerings or found buyers in a series of deals each worth some $1 billion or more.

In March, game developer King Digital Entertainment PLC listed in New York. Online restaurant delivery company Just Eat PLC listed on the London Stock Exchange in April. Other listings include software maker Zendesk Inc. and French ad firm Criteo SA.

Another investment, California-based Arista Networks, Inc., said last week it plans an IPO on the New York Stock Exchange.

Meanwhile, Monsanto Co. bought data-science company The Climate Corp. and Japan's SoftBank Corp. bought a 51% stake in Finnish gaming company Supercell Oy.

Not all of these "exits" have gone smoothly for new investors buying into them. King Digital's shares slid sharply after its IPO on March 26. Ben Holmes, an Index Ventures general partner based in London, said the stock market isn't accurately valuing King.

"The fundamentals of all of these businesses are incredibly strong and they're in enormous markets that are just growing and growing," said Saul Klein, another partner at the firm.

Index has so far focused on finding companies in the areas of mobile consumer services and applications, enterprise infrastructure, networks, and financial services. Mr. Holmes said new areas of interest include controls and sensors for mobile phones, artificial intelligence, and robotics.

The latest fund is Index's seventh early-stage fund and part of a total  ¬3 billion raised by Index since its inception in 1996. It typically keeps between 20 and 30 investors in every fund, almost all of them existing investors.

Mr. Klein said that the firm's investment approach in terms of geography is balanced between Europe, Israel, and the U.S.

"While obviously Silicon Valley and the U.S. still looms large in terms of creating very, very big successful tech companies," Mr. Klein said, "we have really seen in the last 12 months this wave of businesses that have grown up either in Europe or in Israel really starting to achieve what is becoming a sort of entrepreneurial threshold of significance."

pict045.png

How fund managers spend your money

By Norma Cohen 
2,003 words
6 June 2014
18:42
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

In Turney Duff's account of his drug-addled and alcohol-soaked days picking shares for a hedge fund, recounted with great relish in The Buy Side, a broker pitching for trade asks Mr Duff what he can do to get more of his business.

"Well, you can start by taking me to Vegas," he responds. So they do. Not just Las Vegas, but Florida too. They charter private jets and attend sporting events where the thrill of the match is amplified by cocaine and parties where sex is on call.

Why are brokers falling over themselves to offer such expensive inducements to Mr Duff? The answer is simple: each block of shares the hedge fund buys or sells carries a commission. That commission does not come out of Mr Duff's pocket, nor does it come out of the pocket of his employer. Ultimately, it comes out of your pocket.

Amid all the debate about what investors should be told about fees, transaction costs are the elephant in the room. They are not part of the two most commonly-used cost disclosures, the annual management charge (AMC) or the total expense ratio (TER, sometimes also known as the ongoing charges figure or OCF). But by some estimates, they may account for as much as a third of what leaks from investors' portfolios in fees and costs.

"There's a lot of evidence that about 30 per cent of the cost of running a pension fund is lost through trading and other costs," said Richard Balarkas, who runs an independent consultancy that analyses market structure and execution services. "Transaction costs are the murky, murky part of the industry."

Of course, those buying investment funds do "see" transaction costs, in the form of a lower investment return than they might have otherwise had. But because this is rarely spelt out clearly, most investors - even professionals running large corporate pension schemes - are in the dark about it.

A 2013 survey of fund managers by actuaries Lane, Clark & Peacock, whose clients are large UK pension schemes, found that only a third of fund managers disclose transaction fees. LCP noted that managers of funds of hedge funds were particularly unwilling to disclose these extra costs. "It seems that such providers would rather keep investors in the dark about the true cost incurred to manage assets."

-------------------------------------------

What are transaction costs?

Even defining what constitutes transaction costs can be a challenge, but experts broadly divide these into two categories: explicit and implicit costs. Among the explicit costs are things such as stamp duty, exchange fees and brokerage commissions. Some explicit fees, such as charges for custody (holding investments in ringfenced accounts, to protect investors in the event of the provider getting into financial difficulty) and legal and audit services, are included in the TER.

Brokerage fees, which provided the fuel for Mr Duff's hard partying, are easy to define but far from easy to measure. That's partly because their cost does not just reflect the price of buying and selling shares or bonds, but a whole range of other things, such as research or preferential access to corporate clients.

And brokerage commissions are only part of trading costs. "It's not talked about much, but implicit costs can be much more significant than explicit costs," said Yossi Brandes, head of European analytics research at ITG, a company that specialises in measuring them.

Brokers act as introducers for new companies coming to the stock market. A fund manager who puts a lot of business their way is more likely to secure a large tranche of new stock in an IPO. A 2008 study by academics at Babson College and the Universities of Georgia and Missouri found clear evidence that fund managers engage in "churning" - unnecessary stock trading - to gain a leg up on broker allocations of initial public offerings.

-------------------------------------------

Implementation shortfalls

Implicit costs are not only harder to see and measure, but also harder to define. Broadly, the implicit cost is the gap between the price of a security - shares, bonds, traded funds - at the time the investment decision to buy or sell is made and the price change over the course of fulfilling an order. For securities in foreign currencies, it also includes the difference between the rate at which that currency is bought and sold.

One of the trickiest issues is "implementation shortfall". Typically, as an order is executed, other market participants will adjust the price at which they are offering that security to reflect either the rising demand as shares are bought or falling demand as they are sold. There is often a time lag between the point at which a manager decides to buy or sell a share and the time the transaction is complete. That price change is called the implementation shortfall and is almost impossible to measure accurately.

"If you are a large institution and you need to buy a stock, it will drive the price up," Mr Brandes said, adding that such moves can be long-lasting. In that case, investment returns are lower than they might be otherwise.

In trying to understand some of these hidden costs, some broad rules of thumb apply. For example, fund managers buying less liquid securities will see a bigger adverse price movement when they trade than they might when buying more widely traded shares, and securities purchased in less liquid currencies will also prove more expensive than those denominated in, say, US dollars.

Another rule of thumb is that fund managers who buy and sell shares within a portfolio frequently will lose more of their investment returns to frictional trading costs than a manager who trades infrequently.

So-called "portfolio turnover" can vary widely between funds. It can be very low at long-only equity funds, but tends to be higher for bond funds, which must continuously replace maturing holdings.

But size matters, too. "Portfolio turnover matters less for big funds that can negotiate lower transaction costs," says Hugues Gillibert, chief executive of Fitz Partners, a consultancy. He says a big fund with high turnover but low transaction charges could cost investors less overall than a small fund with low turnover.

But portfolio turnover is one reason investors in "passive" funds that track an index are far less likely to be adversely affected by hidden charges than those investing in actively managed portfolios.

Actuarial consultants Hymans Robertson recently sought to quantify the difference in cost between managing various investments in index trackers versus having them actively managed. Its report prompted the Department of Communities and Local Government to urge switching almost half the 178bn Local Government Pension Fund into index trackers.

Even for the highly liquid UK market, the cost differential is huge. The scheme has roughly equal sums of about 25bn invested in trackers and active management. But total fees on the actively managed portion are nearly four times those on the passive portfolios, and there is no evidence that their returns are any better. One factor eating into returns of the actively managed funds are the hidden transaction costs.

-------------------------------------------

Regulators shine a light

Regulators and the industry are moving to require that some of these hidden costs be shown to investors. The main driver for that is the fact that, increasingly, households will have to rely on their own investment decisions for their retirement income. It is becoming more important to ensure that as much as possible of each pound invested is working for the saver.

"We have a huge, looming pensions crisis in Europe and this is an effort to address a problem that is spiralling out of control," said Rebecca Healey, senior research analyst at Tabb Group, a consulting firm specialising in trading market analysis.

The second part of the EU's markets in financial instruments directive (Mifid) will require fund managers to spell out some of these soft costs. "The information on all costs and associated charges must include information relating to both investment and ancillary services, including the cost of advice, where relevant . . ." the directive states. It will be implemented from the fourth quarter of 2016.

Investors can be heartened by the fact that regulators are moving towards illuminating the darker corners of the market, and curtailing the apocryphal golf days and Test match tickets. But for those paying unseen costs today, those moves may still seem rather too slow.

-------------------------------------------

The price of research

Many fund management groups market themselves on the depth of their in-house research, the cost of which is incorporated in their charges. But investment banks also employ analysts to produce research for distribution to clients. This is paid for indirectly via trading commissions.

The Financial Conduct Authority estimates that funds spent 1.5bn a year on external research and 500m on preferential access to senior company managers. Both costs are met through brokerage commissions.

Many experts feel the cost of external research - while legitimate - should be borne by the fund manager and incorporated into the TER, rather than paid for indirectly via opaque commissions.

The regulator agrees; in November 2013, it announced a crackdown on "cash for access" in particular.

A separate report in December 2013 suggested that much of the research that investment bank analysts produce is not even read by fund managers.

-------------------------------------------

A common standard on disclosure

How much information should be disclosed to investors, and in what format, has become a hot issue for the funds industry.

The Investment Management Association, which represents open-ended fund providers in the UK, says it is "absolutely" in favour of better disclosure.

"We have huge sympathy with someone who wants to know how much of the money that they invest is actually put to work," says Guy Sears, director of risk, compliance and legal affairs. The difficulty is agreeing a uniform method for measuring and disclosing costs that is comparable across the market. "We're interested in making sure that the disclosure is uniform and there is clarity on methodology."

Campaigners such as Alan and Gina Miller, who initiated the True & Fair Campaign, say that only a single all-encompassing figure in pounds and pence will do, while the City regulator has urged managers to use the same figure across all marketing literature.

The IMA has committed to provide a standard framework for implicit costs, which would result in a "pounds and pence" figure, but its critics argue that the proposals do not go far enough and that details of the all-in cost will only appear in a fund's annual report - a document most investors don't bother to read.

While fund management groups may protest that it is too difficult to provide costs data to consumers in a consistent format, that does not mean they are not measuring it themselves.

Mr Sears says a very large number of providers are using some form of transaction cost analysis software which constantly monitors the cost of trading for internal purposes. But he adds that there is no standardised methodology across clients or vendors for what is measured and how.

"There is a risk of 'apples to oranges' problems if comparing asset manager A with B."

Many in the funds industry argue that transaction costs of fractions of a per cent are very minor, and that what really matters is performance net of fees. But across an industry where average performance is barely any better than what could be delivered by an index tracker, costs matter - especially over the long term.

A recent report by the Pensions Institute noted that up to 85 per cent of investing costs may be hidden.

And advocates of better disclosure are unmoved. "The question to ask the manager of your fund is 'What would the investment return have been at the end of the year if you did not trade a single share?'" Mr Balarkas says.

He recalls a regulator posing just that question to a room full of fund managers - and being greeted with silence.

European Markets Await ECB Policy Announcement; Central Bank Widely Expected to Cut All Its Main Interest Rates

By Tommy Stubbington and Josie Cox

The Wall Street Journal Online

Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

European markets stuck to their recent holding pattern Thursday while investors awaited the European Central Bank's policy announcement.

The euro, which has weakened since ECB President Mario Draghi last month suggested that easing measures could be on their way in June, was steady against the dollar at $1.3610.

The central bank is widely expected to cut all its main interest rates at Thursday's meeting, including pushing its deposit rate into negative territory for the first time. Some analysts also expect further action such as steps to boost liquidity or a package of cheap loans to banks.

"For recent euro weakness to extend further after today's meeting, the ECB will likely now have to meet high investor expectations and perhaps even hint that more easing is likely later this year," said Lee Hardman, a currency analyst at Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi UFJ.

Investors will also be looking out for signals from Mr. Draghi of future steps particularly the possibility that the ECB could launch a program of large-scale asset purchases if inflation continues at its current sluggish pace.

Data earlier in the week showed consumer price growth in the currency bloc slowed to just 0.5% annually in May.

A survey conducted by Barclays shows that just over 46% of its clients questioned expect the ECB to cut rates and announce targeted credit easing.

In equity markets, the Stoxx Europe 600 index was little changed in early trade.

Outside the euro zone, London's FTSE 100 slipped 0.2%. The Bank of England also makes its monthly interest rate decision Thursday and is widely expected to keep policy on hold.

In the U.S., S&P 500 futures were little changed, with investors eyeing the government's monthly employment report due Friday, which is expected show nonfarm payroll growth of 210,000.

On Wednesday, data compiled by Automatic Data Processing and Moody'sAnalytics showed that 179,000 private-sector jobs were added in May, falling short of expectations of a 210,000 increase. Changes in futures don't always accurately predict market moves after the opening bell.

Back in European equity markets, Smith & Nephew shares climbed sharply after Medtronic Inc. was reported by Bloomberg to be considering an offer for the U.K. medical-device maker.

In a further sign of the market for mergers and acquisitions reviving, German household consumer products maker Henkel AG & Co. said is would pay  ¬940 million ($1.27 billion) in cash for French company Spotless GroupSAS in a move aimed at strengthening its position in Western Europe.

According to Dealogic data from this week, the volume of global consumer-sector M&A stands at $26.6 billion year-to-date, already marking a robust 89% rise on the same period last year.

Elsewhere, shares in online retailer Asos slumped by more than 40% after issuing a profit warning, saying increased levels of promotional activity have hit margins. Year-to-date, that marks a more than 47% tumble in the stock price.

In commodities markets, gold was down 0.1% at $1,243.40 an ounce, while Brent crude oil was down 0.3% at $108.07 a barrel.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- How to FIX the 401(k) --- Five suggestions for giving workers a more-secure retirement

By Anna Prior 
1,480 words
3 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
R1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

The 401(k) is in need of a tuneup.

Originally designed to supplement pension plans, 401(k)s have become the primary retirement-savings vehicle for many employees. But critics say the plans are plagued by a variety of ills, including high fees that can eat into investment returns, as well as certain advisers who may not have a legal obligation to work in the best interests of plan participants.

"The system is broken," says Monique Morrissey, an economist at the left-leaning Economic Policy Institute in Washington. "The best way to encourage people to save more is to give them something that will make them confident that if they put more money away, they will have sufficient and secure retirement income."

Here are five suggestions to strengthen the traditional 401(k).

Simplify fee disclosure

High fees are at the root of many of the criticisms lobbed at 401(k)s.

According to BrightScope, a San Diego analytics firm that rates retirement plans, average total plan fees for large 401(k) plans with more than $1 billion in total assets are about 0.34% of assets annually, or $340 a year for an account with a $100,000 balance. However, at smaller plans with $1 million to $100 million in assets, the average is 1.16%, or $1,160 a year. Total plan fees include various administrative costs as well as the expenses built into the underlying investment choices.

It can be difficult for small businesses and plan participants to get a handle on what fees and expenses are attached to their 401(k) plans because the numbers are often buried in the dense, complex and lengthy disclosure documents that became the norm after the U.S. Labor Department in 2012 required plan providers to reveal detailed information about their services and the compensation they receive.

The Labor Department in March proposed new rules designed to make navigation of these documents easier, citing concern that small businesses in particular might be having trouble.

In addition to plan administrative costs, the expense ratios associated with individual funds also can hurt workers' returns, which has led to calls for simpler, easy-to-read disclosure of these expenses, too. Some have envisioned a disclosure similar to a new car's gas-mileage label or energy-cost labels on appliances, says John Rekenthaler, vice president of research at Morningstar Inc.

The left-leaning Center for American Progress, for example, wants funds to carry a warning label explaining how high fees eat into savings, and showing how the fund's fees compare with a group of comparable, low-fee offerings.

"The ability to see that fee metric in a way that signals to them whether they are buying a low-cost fund or a higher-cost fund would be incredibly valuable," says Jennifer Erickson, the director of competitiveness and economic growth at American Progress.

Require plans to offer low-cost index funds

Some critics say that along with better fee disclosure, 401(k) plans need to include more low-cost index mutual funds and exchange-traded funds.

According to Morningstar, the average expense ratio for an index mutual fund is 0.75%, or $7.50 in annual fees for every $1,000 invested, compared with 1.25% for actively managed mutual funds. ETFs are even cheaper, with an average expense ratio of 0.59%.

The difference in the average expense ratio of actively managed mutual funds and ETFs translates into an extra $8,711 in fees and forgone earnings over 30 years, assuming an initial investment of $10,000 and a 6% annual rate of return, according to a calculator from the Securities and Exchange Commission.

The easiest way for employees to control their own costs is to pick a diversified set of investment options with lower expense ratios, says Ms. Erickson.

Larger employers are more likely to offer low-cost index funds in their 401(k) plans than smaller employers, says Morningstar's Mr. Rekenthaler. Employees, however, can always ask their employer to add index funds and ETFs to the company's 401(k) plan, he says.

Require plan features that nudge people to save more

The 2006 Pension Protection Act made it easier for employers to automatically enroll workers into workplace savings plans, and many in the defined-contribution industry have applauded the practice.

Vanguard Group, one of the biggest U.S. 401(k) plan administrators, says that among companies that use its plans, the employee participation rate rose to 76% in 2012 from 66% in 2006, likely reflecting the adoption of automatic enrollment by larger plan sponsors.

Almost half of 401(k) plans now have an automatic-enrollment feature, according to the Plan Sponsor Council of America. Some employers automatically escalate workers' contributions each year. As of 2012, nearly 40% of employer plans with an automatic enrollment feature included an auto-escalation feature, said the council, while about 18% of plans included an option for employees to opt in for auto-escalation.

The challenge, experts say, is coming up with automatic features that don't inadvertently dissuade workers from saving even more. For instance, if employers have automatic enrollment with auto-escalation, they shouldn't cap the escalating contribution at a relatively low level like 8%, says Jack VanDerhei, research director at the nonprofit Employee Benefit Research Institute, because it might lead employees to believe they can't contribute more.

Mr. VanDerhei also says plan sponsors should encourage new hires not to default to a low starting level if they were contributing more at a former job.

"New employees should be reminded that they've escalated contributions over the last so-many years, so they don't revert to a small percentage at a new employer," says Mr. VanDerhei.

Have those providing guidance be fiduciaries

Fiduciaries are required by law to act in the best interest of their clients when giving certain investment advice.

While those responsible for choosing the specific investment options offered within 401(k) plans (usually the employer or someone they hire) are subject to fiduciary standards under the Employee Retirement Income Security Act, those who provide assistance or suggestions to the employer or to individual plan participants aren't necessarily subject to the same rules.

For instance, a broker-dealer may approach an employer about setting up a 401(k) plan, and in that process suggest certain models or groups of funds to offer within the plan. Under current laws, that transaction isn't subject to fiduciary standards, though the employer offering the plan is.

"There are people working with smaller and midsize plans that are providing advisory services in various forms, but aren't calling themselves fiduciaries," says Christopher Jones, chief investment officer for Financial Engines Inc., a Sunnyvale, Calif. company and fiduciary that provides advice and money management to 401(k) participants.

The Labor Department is expected to unveil in January a proposal to update the fiduciary definition, a move supported by consumer groups including AARP, the Consumer Federation of America and the Pension Rights Center.

The potential proposal faces opposition from some in the brokerage industry.

By taking broker-dealers' suggestions for different models off the table, it takes away a tool and potentially drives up costs for small employers, says Kent Mason, a lawyer and partner at Davis & Harman LLP in Washington who represents brokerages and other financial firms.

Start a national 401(k) plan for small employers

While some argue that 401(k) plans at large employers have made improvements, small-employer plans have continued to struggle with high fees that can get passed on to employees.

That's partly because legal and administrative costs can weigh heavily on plans that cover fewer workers. "The 401(k) is a tax-code accident that wasn't written from the top down for retirement, and therefore isn't built for small companies," says Mr. Rekenthaler.

Though the recent focus has been on the individual retirement accounts called "myRAs" proposed by President Barack Obama earlier this year, Mr. Rekenthaler says a national platform for employer-sponsored defined-contribution plans would also help bring better 401(k) plans to American workers.

A national 401(k) plan would allow small companies to benefit from economies of scale by pooling their resources and reining in individual costs, he says. He adds that such a platform would also lift the costs associated with fiduciary responsibility away from individual small employers since the funds in the national plan would be picked by a government entity.

Having individualized plans at small companies just isn't cost-effective, says Mr. Rekenthaler.

---

Ms. Prior is a Wall Street Journal reporter in New York. Email: anna.prior@wsj.com.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Breakneck business-cycle fix for Schroders; Fund focus

By Bradley Gerrard 
530 words
2 June 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
13
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Manager keeping just five stocks in European fund's overhaul, finds Bradley Gerrard

Up-and-coming investment manager James Sym has retained just five stocks in a breakneck overhaul of Schroders' 1bn European Alpha Plus fund, as he switches the strategy to "business-cycle investing".

Schroders announced that Mr Sym was taking over the fund from industry veteran Leon Howard-Spink just three weeks ago. But the rising-star investor says he is already "80 per cent" through implementation of the new approach, which evaluates companies in terms of how they perform at different points in the business cycle.

The manager change came after Mr Howard-Spink, formerly of Jupiter, gained just 3.3 per cent on the fund in the year to the end of April, versus a sector average of 14.5 per cent, according to FE Analytics, the data provider.

Mr Sym joined Cazenove Capital - bought by Schroders in 2013 - as an analyst in 2007 before being promoted to co-manager of its European Income fund in 2012 alongside business-cycle expert Chris Rice. The business-cycle investing approach was founded by former Cazenove manager Tim Russell.

Mr Rice quit Cazenove during the Schroders takeover, which resulted in Mr Sym taking the reins of the 309m income fund. Mr Sym has now been tasked with implementing the business-cycle approach on Schroders' much larger European Alpha Plus fund.

"My starting point was not what is in the portfolio, but [to ask] what are the best 40 companies I can find to make money," says the manager. "I am doing away with almost the entire portfolio. The transition process is under way and 80 per cent of the portfolio is already changed."

Mr Sym says there were just three stocks that he and Mr Howard-Spink both had in the portfolios. In the transition process he "took a second look" at two other companies and decided to keep those as well.

"One of the real challenges will be managing a bigger fund, but what has delighted and surprised me most is that clients have left their money in," he says. "There is going to be quite a substantial amount of assets to run."

Mr Sym says "early-cycle" stocks had driven the returns on his income fund in recent years. However, he says many of these types of stocks have now priced in much of the recovery.

He cites a Spanish TV company he bought last year as an example. Its price-to-earnings ratio almost tripled from the time he purchased it.

The manager adds that as the business cycle moves into the "expansion" phase he is focusing on stocks that tend to fare well in this type of environment. As such he is considering a brick manufacturer in Austria, whose share price he expects to "double or triple" because of low earnings expectations from the market.

"I am not going to get all of them right but I have got lots of bets where I can make multiples on initial investments," he says.

Bradley Gerrard is news editor at Investment Adviser

Special
