Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. stocks were uneven in choppy trading Wednesday, as investors weighed a mixed bag of economic data.

Investors were awaiting the Federal Reserve's monetary-policy statement due in the afternoon.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose eight points, less than 0.1%, to 16544 in midday trading. The index had climbed within seven points of its Dec. 31 all-time record of 16576.66 early in the session.

The S&P 500 index edged up less than 0.1% to 1879, and the Nasdaq Composite Index slipped eight points, or 0.2%, to 4095.

U.S. gross domestic product grew just 0.1% in the first quarter, far shy of the 1.1% growth economists had expected from the fourth quarter's 2.6%. Automatic Data Processing and Moody'sAnalytics reported that 220,000 jobs were created in April, above expectations for a rise of 210,000. Many view the report as a preview of the government's monthly jobs report due Friday.

The Institute for Supply Management's Chicago-area purchasing-managers index for April rose more than expected.

Economists were expecting the Fed to announce a further trimming of its bond-purchase program, a process known as tapering, by $10 billion a month to $45 billion.

Twitter's stock slumped 9.6% after the company reported late Tuesday that its user growth in the first quarter fell below Wall Street expectations. Shares fell despite first-quarter adjusted earnings and revenue that beat forecasts.

Some other consumer Internet stocks were also lower. LinkedIn lost 1.3%, and Yelp, which was to report results after the close, fell 4.2%.

EBay shed 4.9% after the company's first-quarter adjusted earnings topped forecasts but it provided a second-quarter outlook that was below current projections.

In deals news, Pepco Holdings rallied 17% after agreeing to be acquired by rival electricity and gas utility Exelon in an all-cash deal valued at $6.8 billion. Exelon fell 4.3%.

European markets inched lower, with the Stoxx Europe 600 easing 0.1% to 337.89. For the month, the Stoxx 600 rose 1.1%.

BNP Paribas dropped 3.2% after the French bank warned that it may pay more than the $1.1 billion it had set aside last quarter to cover potential fines stemming from alleged U.S. sanctions violations.

Alstom surged 9.3% as the board of the French conglomerate backed a review of a roughly $12 billion bid from General Electric for Alstom's energy business.

GlaxoSmithKline fell 2% as the drug maker posted a 30% drop in first-quarter profit.

Pandit takes chairman's role at TGG

By Tom Braithwaite in New York 
380 words
1 May 2014
00:38
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Vikram Pandit, the former chief executive of Citigroup, has become chairman of TGG Group, a consulting company co-founded by Steven Levitt, who co-wrote the pop economics book Freakonomics .

Promising to harness two popular themes - behavioural economics and "big data" - TGG, short for The Greatest Good, pitches itself to companies as a way to "improve performance, better serve customers, hire and retain the best talent and reduce cost".

Andrew Rosenfield, chief executive, said he met Mr Pandit when consulting for Citi and persuaded him to join after he was ousted from the bank in 2012.

"He would have been an extremely successful academic, which is an unusual characteristic for someone who became the CEO of one of the world's largest companies," said Mr Rosenfield, who is a managing partner at Guggenheim Partners, the asset manager.

Since then Hamid Biglari, former head of emerging markets at Citi, Lewis Kaden, former vice-chairman, and Shannon Bell, former head of communications, have also joined. TGG's office on Madison Avenue in New York is 200m from Citi's Park Avenue headquarters.

"In certain areas where we work clients may wish to have their expertise as part of the process," said Mr Rosenfield of his Citi alumni.

In an internal memo to staff, which he said included about 50 young economists and statisticians, Mr Rosenfield spared no buzzword, saying the company was about "unpacking the knowledge hidden in 'big data'. We also exploit the newest thinking in behavioural economics and are the leading experts in designing 'nudges' and 'choice architectures'."

Nudge is a book co-written by Cass Sunstein, who is also listed as a partner at TGG, which argues human behaviour can be altered better by suggestion than instruction, and whose adherents have included David Cameron, the UK prime minister.

"Big data" is the ability to analyse vast amounts of stored information.

Mr Rosenfield said TGG had a policy of charging only for success. "We don't charge if we don't deliver value," he said. But that also meant it did not make outsized returns when advice was highly successful - "That seems sort of unsporting," he said - which is why the company is founding its venture arm.

Error By BofA Scuttles Buyback

By Christina Rexrode, Dan Fitzpatrick and Ryan Tracy 
1,050 words
29 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

An apparent communications blunder inside Bank of America Corp . forced the lender to shelve a plan to buy back shares and boost its dividend for the first time since 2008, another setback for Chief Executive Brian Moynihan 's efforts to escape the long shadow of the financial crisis.

The second-largest U.S. bank by assets Monday said it discovered a mistake in certain figures submitted to the Federal Reserve for the regulator's annual "stress tests" of major U.S. financial institutions. The error leaves the Charlotte, N.C., bank with $4 billion less in capital than it thought it had. The bank had been making the same calculation error since 2009, according to a person close to the bank.

Bank of America brought the mistake to the Fed's attention. The regulator revoked its prior approval of a dividend increase and gave the bank until May 27 to submit a new plan. But the bank said the cash returned to investors in the new plan will be less than in the previous one.

Disappointed shareholders pushed the stock down 6.3%, to $14.95 a share, the biggest one-day plunge since 2012.

The blunder shows the difficulties executives and regulators face in overseeing financial firms that have become more complex and more sprawling since a wave of forced mergers during the financial crisis.

Despite numerous federal regulators in its offices, and a huge apparatus of compliance employees and risk controls, neither Bank of America nor its overseers caught the mistake until last week.

Analysts said the capital shortfall was relatively small, but the mistake sparked concerns about whether Bank of America has enough control over its far-flung businesses.

The news "re-highlights broader questions of too-big-to-manage," said Evercore Group analyst Andrew Marquardt , while also underscoring the Fed's grip on the banking industry.

The problem on this year's test can be tied to a misunderstanding between two internal units about a change in the value of debt securities, said people close to the situation.

One of the units had been created to make sure the bank's capital plan didn't get rejected following a previous stress-test miss in 2011, these people said. The unit's head was promoted to chief risk officer for the bank Thursday.

Mr. Moynihan on Monday tapped another executive to oversee the bank's resubmission to the Fed. Terry Laughlin , the bank's president for strategic initiatives and a trusted ally of Mr. Moynihan's for two decades, also will helm an internal investigation about what went wrong, alongside corporate general auditor Christine Katziff . The bank declined to make Mr. Moynihan, Mr. Laughlin or Ms. Katziff available for comment.

A month ago, Bank of America appeared to be putting much of its financial-crisis woes behind it when it announced the Fed had granted it permission to raise its quarterly dividend and buy back stock.

"We are pleased," Mr. Moynihan said in a release on March 26, the day the Fed offered its initial approval.

Regulators in 2008 had forced Bank of America to drop its dividend payout to a penny per common share after it took a double helping of bailout loans to absorb its acquisition of troubled securities firm Merrill Lynch & Co .

The dividend became a major priority for Mr. Moynihan after he took over from his predecessor, Kenneth Lewis , in 2010.

The bank still has enough high-quality capital to meet regulatory requirements. Bank of America said the change has no impact on previously announced earnings results.

One former shareholder, John Moore , of Charlotte, N.C., said he is glad he sold the last of his shares earlier this month following a first-quarter loss fueled by huge legal expenses. "After a while, you finally give up," said the chairman of Moore Companies, a commercial real-estate investment firm.

Bank of America executives had been using the wrong calculations since 2009, but the problem didn't come to light until the last week when one finance employee discovered that numbers provided to an internal unit known as the enterprise capital management group were incorrect, said people familiar with the situation.

This is the second time in four tries Bank of America has flubbed the test. In March 2011, executives were taken aback when the Fed denied a modest dividend increase planned for the second half of that year.

The Fed has had problems of its own with how the stress tests are handled. This year it had to restate some of its initial analysis of big banks' capital levels in a downturn after finding what it called "inconsistencies" in some of the results. Banks for years have complained about premature test leaks, misunderstandings about when certain information could be disclosed and wide gaps between the banks' calculations and the Fed's.

The fact that Bank of America , rather than its regulator, detected its latest problem highlights how, despite a range of new risk-management requirements imposed since the financial crisis, bank supervisors remain reliant on megabanks to keep tabs on their own sprawling operations.

"Note that the Fed didn't catch this," ISI Group analyst Glenn Schorr said in a note Monday.

Bank of America 's disclosure is "a reflection of the work-in-progress nature" of the stress tests, said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the policy-analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc .

While the Fed checks the capital data banks submit for irregularities, the regulator doesn't examine each of the thousands of data points it receives, and Bank of America 's submission doesn't appear to have raised red flags. Still, Fed staff are reviewing how they study banks' submissions of regulatory capital data to see if improvements are needed, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Sam Pappas, CEO at Mystic Asset Management in Warwick, R.I., said he planned to hold on to his Bank of America shares.

"It's positive that Bank of America found the mistake and reported it to the Fed," Mr. Pappas said. "But it also makes you wonder what else could be lurking."

McGraw Hill Financial Results Helped By Strength at Indexes Division; Indexes Division Helped Offset Declines in Standard & Poor's Ratings Services

Timothy W. Martin

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

McGraw Hill Financial Inc. said first-quarter earnings from continuing operations grew 9%, as strength in its fast-growing indexes division helped offset declines in Standard & Poor's Ratings Services.

The New York-based company earned $248 million, or 89 cents a share, versus $735 million, or $2.59 a share, in the year-prior quarter, which included a $612 million gain from the sale of discontinued operations.

Excluding special items, earnings from continuing operations were 89 cents a share, up from 80 cents a year earlier. McGraw Hill's first-quarter revenue rose 4.7% to $1.24 billion. Analysts polled by Thomson Reuters expected adjusted earnings of 87 cents a share on revenue of $1.24 billion.

The company also reaffirmed its full-year earnings outlook of $3.75 to $3.85 a share.

McGraw Hill shares were down 1% in early afternoon trade at $74.33, putting the stock down 4.9% for 2014.

The S&P Ratings segment reported revenue growth of 1% to $569 million and operating profits fell 4%, as global debt issuance started slowly this year. Overall company revenue was driven by financial information divisions S&P Dow Jones Indices and Platts, which grew 18% and 14%, respectively.

The two units also represent a bigger slice of McGraw Hill's profits: Some 48% of operating profits were derived from non-rating businesses, up from 41% during the same quarter a year ago.

"Bond issuance was erratic during the quarter, but overall it was weak," said McGraw Hill Chief Executive Douglas L. Peterson during Tuesday's earnings call with analysts. Excluding sovereign debt, the total number of bond issues declined by 27% compared with the first quarter of 2013, he added. The indexes unit and Platts, which offers analytics for global commodity markets, "have grown into major contributors," Mr. Peterson said.

Jack Callahan, McGraw Hill's chief financial officer, said on the call that S&P Ratings' performance this year could be "flat to modestly down." But growth could pick up in the third and fourth quarters, he added.

"Indices was the standout," Douglas Arthur, an analyst with Evercore Group LLC, wrote in a note to investors following the quarterly report.

McGraw Hill's first-quarter profits were "driven by expenses, as revenues were weaker than we anticipated," Patrick O'Shaughnessy, an analyst with Raymond James & Associates, Inc., wrote in a note to investors following the quarterly report. The company's operating margins of 34.8% beat Mr. O'Shaughnessy's estimate of 33.1%. But revenues in McGraw Hill's four major divisions each failed to meet projections, he added.

Formerly called McGraw-Hill Cos., the company has streamlined its operations, selling its education unit to private-equity firm Apollo Global Management LLC for $2.4 billion in 2013. It has also sold off trade publication Aviation Week and a broadcasting unit.

The company said last month it plans to sell its construction division, which provides commercial-real-estate information to developers and manufacturers. The company previously said it would also consider finding a partner to run the construction business.

McGraw Hill also said it would target $100 million in cost cuts over the next three years, focusing on its real-estate portfolio and consolidating technology, data acquisition and finance costs

S&P, which is facing a $5 billion fraud lawsuit brought by the federal government, said it had $1.5 billion in cash and equivalents at the end of the first quarter.

More broadly, the company has seen some cases filed against S&P dismissed, leaving it with "a couple dozen nongovernment cases," Mr. Peterson said.

The U.S. lawsuit alleges S&P ignored its own standards when rating mortgage bonds that eventually were downgraded during the financial crisis. A trial date has not been set, but both sides have indicated an acceptable start would be September 2015.

The other suits referred to by Mr. Peterson are largely civil actions, filed by private plaintiffs, challenging whether S&P's mortgage ratings were made in good faith.

S&P and other ratings firms had been expected to post robust profits for the latest quarter as they continue to benefit from a global spike in bond sales. S&P rival Moody's Corp. said last week its earnings rose 16% from the year-ago quarter, exceeding analysts' expectations with a boost from its analytics division.

Power Shifts to Pick at Morgan Stanley

By Justin Baer 
1,183 words
28 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Standing before a roomful of clients in Morgan Stanley's New York headquarters, Edward "Ted" Pick couldn't hold back tears.

His voice wavering, the investment bank's equities boss thanked money managers from firms such as Wellington Management Co. and Viking Global Investors during the June 2009 meeting for not abandoning Morgan Stanley even as it teetered near collapse during the financial crisis.

"The folks in this room stuck with us during the worst time in the history of this firm," he said, according to people familiar with the matter. "We're open for business."

Five years later, Morgan Stanley's success in the postcrisis era rests, in part, on how Mr. Pick and his business perform.

The equities division puts buyers and sellers together in the $60 trillion global stock market, while also handling stock derivatives, big blocks of shares and hedge-fund relationships. Once overshadowed by flashier businesses at the New York bank, Mr. Pick's unit has emerged from the crisis as one of its biggest growth engines.

At a time when debt securities, currencies and commodities businesses muddle through a slump in client activity, and new regulations threaten to rein in fixed-income trading well beyond the current malaise, stock trading is taking on more importance across Wall Street.

The shift has played to Morgan Stanley's strengths and helped catapult Mr. Pick, an executive known for his fierce devotion to the firm where he has spent his entire career, into a leading role.

In the precrisis years, when fixed-income trading boomed, and for a brief stint in 2009 when demand for fixed-income products exploded after the crisis, Morgan Stanley was an also-ran to the likes of Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. But as debt trading subsided and the stock market began motoring, spurring a flurry of listings, Morgan Stanley's fortunes have changed.

The firm posted a 16% jump in stock-trading revenue in the first three months of the year, snatching the crown as revenue leader from Goldman for the second time in three quarters.

Morgan Stanley has repositioned itself since the crisis under Chief Executive James Gorman, moving away from riskier trading and toward steadier businesses such as wealth management and equities.

Mr. Pick, who declined to be interviewed for this article, was one of the people Mr. Gorman charged with executing that turnaround. A former capital-markets banker who spent most of his career taking clients public, the 45-year-old executive was reassigned in 2009 to lead an equities trading arm gravely wounded by the crisis.

Under the direction of his mentor, Colm Kelleher, who now heads the firm's securities business, Mr. Pick and his team remade the prime brokerage, which provides services and loans to hedge funds. They also invested heavily in electronic-trading platforms and led Morgan Stanley's traders back to the riskier corners of the business, including derivatives and block trades.

The revival, also engineered by lieutenants such as top sales executive Richard Portogallo and head trader Sam Kellie-Smith, has put Mr. Pick's name in the mix for bigger jobs at the firm, executives said, even if no promotion is imminent. He remains very close to Mr. Kelleher, and in his former role was among the bankers who secured key capital infusions for the firm during the crisis.

Yet Mr. Pick's current mission is far from complete. Competitors such as J.P. Morgan and UBS AG also are charging hard in equities, and Goldman remains a formidable foe whose executives loathe finishing second to Morgan Stanley in anything. Morgan Stanley lags behind some of its peers, including Goldman, in derivatives and block trades.

There are also no assurances the stock-trading renaissance will continue. The debate over the role of high-frequency traders could lead to new rules that crimp profit. Stocks could fall out of favor. The market also remains at risk to technology glitches that have roiled investors in recent years.

Colleagues say Mr. Pick is up for the task. Described as passionate and intense, he is skilled at dissecting the possible outcomes of a decision and imposing his process onto those who work for him. "You better be on your game" during meetings, one colleague said. He isn't hard to read, and will destroy ill-formed arguments, the colleague said.

Five-foot-nine and trim, with light brown hair that frames a prominent brow, Mr. Pick was born in New York. At age three, he moved to Caracas, where his father ran a petrochemical business, before returning with his family five years later to live on Long Island.

In April 2009, Morgan Stanley tapped Mr. Pick to be co-head of stock sales and trading from his capital markets post.

Mr. Pick had started at the firm in 1990 as an investment-banking analyst, then returned after collecting a master's degree in business administration from Harvard. He spent most of the next 15 years in the equity capital-markets unit, which takes companies public and manages their stock sales. Mr. Pick helped manage some of the biggest stock sales of the era, including Google Inc.'s auction and China Construction Bank's IPO.

When he took over the struggling equities division, Mr. Pick sought to shore up relationships with clients and extol the firm's virtues.

"He bleeds Morgan Stanley blue," said Roberto Mignone, a hedge-fund manager whose almost $2 billion firm, Bridger Management, continued to do business with Morgan Stanley in the wake of the crisis.

Hedge-fund clients pulled more than half their money from Morgan Stanley between October 2008 and March 2009, people familiar with the matter said.

Mr. Pick rolled out a strategy called the "nine boxes," a matrix of three businesses -- trading in stocks and derivatives as well as prime brokerage -- across three regions, the Americas, Europe and Asia. The goal: improve everywhere.

The firm also started its own electronic-trade order-entry system, called Passport, ahead of many of its rivals, allowing it to move some clients' trades onto a lower-cost platform, people familiar with the matter said.

"It's given them a huge advantage in the market," said Richard Prager, global head of trading and liquidity strategies at BlackRock Inc., the world's largest money manager.

By 2011, Morgan Stanley had overtaken Goldman and others to become the biggest trader of New York Stock Exchange and Nasdaq Stock Market-listed shares, according to an analysis of the exchanges' data by ModernIR, a data-analytics firm. That same year, Mr. Kelleher appointed Mr. Pick sole head of equities.

The unit's transformation was on full display on April 17, when Morgan Stanley reported first-quarter earnings that handily beat analysts' forecasts. During a conference call, Chief Financial Officer Ruth Porat called the prime brokerage "our gem franchise."

In a memo to senior staffers that day, Mr. Pick urged them to "be proud and be humble."

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics -- Upside: Measuring Up the New 'Alternative' Funds

By Joe Light 
880 words
26 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Investors poured nearly $50 billion into "alternative" mutual funds in the past year, according to investment researcher Morningstar.

If only there were an easy way to see how well the funds perform.

The fast-growing category is something of a catchall, combining dozens of funds whose chief similarity is that they don't just buy stocks or bonds, like plain-vanilla funds do.

Some of the funds use strategies common in hedge funds, such as betting that stocks will fall in price or that a proposed company merger or acquisition will be completed. Some combine many different strategies under one umbrella. In general, the funds are supposed to give returns that aren't tied to the performance of stocks or bonds.

The end result is that investors and financial planners -- many of whom allocate money to alternatives in their portfolios -- are hard-pressed to select a good benchmark index against which a fund's performance can be measured.

It is a challenge that even fund companies struggle with.

The prospectus of the Neuberger Berman Absolute Return Multi-Manager Fund, which allocates money to subadvisers using various hedge-fund strategies, compares the fund to three indexes: the S&P 500, the Barclays U.S. Aggregate Bond Index and the HFRX Absolute Return Index, an index of hedge funds.

"Hedge funds can have very different strategies. Sometimes there's no hedge at all," says Matthew Rubin, Neuberger's director of investment strategy.

That can make it inappropriate to compare one particular alternative fund to a general category that includes many different funds, Mr. Rubin says. In an email, he said that the Neuberger fund is more focused on meeting absolute-return and volatility objectives than on beating a single benchmark.

New York Life's MainStay Marketfield Fund has pulled in nearly $13 billion from investors over the past 12 months.

Marketfield is a so-called long/short equity fund, which can both buy stocks and sell them "short," betting that their prices will fall. Because of their ability to short stocks, such funds typically are much less volatile than funds that can only buy stocks.

And yet the Marketfield fund -- and many other alternative funds -- benchmarks itself against the S&P 500, a large-cap stock index that Marketfield, by design, doesn't track.

In 2008, for example, as the S&P 500 tumbled 37%, Marketfield lost only 13%. Last year, when the index rose 32%, including dividends, Marketfield rose only 17%. A New York Life spokeswoman said that no one was available to comment.

If a fund intentionally hedges its exposure to stocks, it isn't really fair to say that the fund did well in 2008 or poorly in 2013, says Jonathan Boersma, head of professional standards and a benchmarking expert at the CFA Institute. The problem is that it wasn't really designed to beat the S&P 500 during up markets in the first place.

One way to see why is to look at a metric called "standard deviation," a statistical measure of the variability in a fund's returns. A higher number means that a fund's or index's returns are more volatile.

Over the past five years, Marketfield has had a standard deviation of 10.5, versus 14 for the S&P 500. Over three years, it has been a little more than half as volatile as the index.

So how do you decide what to compare an alternative fund to?

The first step: Figure out what you might have invested in instead.

For investors new to alternatives, that could mean finding a combination of stocks and bonds -- or stocks and cash -- that comes close to the level of volatility of the alternative fund.

For example, because the Marketfield fund has been half as volatile as the S&P 500 for the past three years, an investor might appropriately expect it to gain -- or lose -- about as much as a portfolio of 50% stocks and 50% cash over that period. The Marketfield fund did better than that, returning 9.5% annually through Thursday, versus 15% for the S&P 500.

Investors can find a fund's standard deviation under the Ratings & Risk tab on the fund's page at Morningstar.com.

Another yardstick: What does the fund manager say his ultimate goal is?

Bruce Feibel, head of performance analytics at BNY Mellon Investment Management, notes that some alternative funds promise to protect investors in down markets. To see if a given fund has done that, an investor can look at its "downside capture ratio" -- also found on the Ratings & Risk page -- which measures the proportion of the stock market's downdrafts that a fund suffers.

A ratio of 50, for example, would mean that the fund only falls by 5% when the S&P 500 falls by 10%.

Ultimately, the fund should do what it says it will do. A "downside protection" fund should protect on the downside, while a fund that promises to deliver in both up and down markets, should show consistently positive returns.

---

Email: joe.light@wsj.com

 Analytics  Business Boosts Moody's Profit

By Timothy W. Martin 
548 words
26 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Moody's Corp. posted record first-quarter profit on Friday, despite a slowdown in global bond issuance earlier this year that cooled some demand for its credit ratings.

Earnings rose 16% from a year earlier, and exceeded analysts' expectations with a boost from Moody'sanalytics division. But overall revenue fell short of Wall Street forecasts.

Moody's established its analytics business in 2007. It sells investors its proprietary analysis, research and market data and now represents about 31% of Moody's Corp.'s overall revenue. Its growth helps guard the company against slowdowns in bond issuance.

The global bond markets -- with record-high new issuance last year amid low interest rates set by central banks -- were quieter in January and February, before turning busier in March.

There are some early indications that issuance could continue to pick up, with 79% of leveraged loans last week being earmarked for corporate deal making, said Moody's Chief Financial Officer Linda Huber during an earnings conference call with analysts.

"That's an interesting statistic that might speak to increased M&A activity," Ms. Huber said in an interview.

An increase in deals could be a boon for ratings firms such as Moody's, because issuance would increase. "You have firms borrowing that otherwise would not be borrowing; that's beneficial for us," Moody's Chief Executive Raymond McDaniel said in an interview.

Moody's reported a quarterly profit of $218 million, or $1 a share, up from $188.4 million, or 83 cents a share, a year earlier. Revenue rose 4.8% to $767.2 million. Analysts polled by Thomson Reuters had projected per-share earnings of 91 cents and revenue of $770 million.

Moody's also backed its full-year earnings outlook of $3.90 to $4 a share. Its shares fell 52 cents to $79.13 on Friday.

Moody's had previously posted quarters with higher profits. But the $218 million tops all of the company's previous first quarters, which has traditionally been a slower time of year for bond issuance.

The strong earnings at Moody's underscore the recent rebound of credit-ratings firms, an industry that drew intense scrutiny from legislators and critics for its role in the financial crisis. This resurgence, industry executives say, is driven by demand for corporate and noninvestment-grade bonds -- not the complex, residential-mortgage securities that got top grades from the firms, but later soured.

Since the crisis, the industry has been subjected to more regulation, changed some of its ratings methodologies and invested in risk management, but it hasn't endured a major overhaul as some critics had hoped.

For the first quarter, the analytics division posted $241.4 million in revenue, a 15% rise from a year earlier, due in part to the firm's acquisition of Amba Investment Services in December.

Still, the bulk of the firm's revenue comes from its Moody's Investors Service debt-rating operation, which posted a revenue increase of less than 1% to $525.8 million during the most recent period. Within the unit, global corporate-finance revenue rose 2.4% to $264.4 million.

World News: Asia: Thailand Holds Rates Steady Despite Slowdown

By Nopparat Chaichalearmmongkol 
507 words
24 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
5
English
Copyright  2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Thailand's central bank held interest rates steady despite a fast decelerating economy caused by continuing political tensions.

The Bank of Thailand kept its key rates at 2%, in line with economists' expectations. Bank officials also said Wednesday they now expect growth this year to come in below 2.7%, already much lower than a forecast of 4.8% before Thailand's opposition launched a series of political protests in the fall. The bank expects an economic contraction in the first three months of 2014 versus the last quarter of 2013.

The central bank already has cut rates twice since antigovernment protesters, led by former opposition lawmaker Suthep Thaugsuban, began street rallies to oust Prime Minister Yingluck Shinawatra. The turmoil remains far from resolved after Thailand's Constitutional Court nullified in March the inconclusive Feb. 2 general election -- a move that further delayed the formation of a new government. New polls are unlikely to take place before July.

The situation has slowed government expenditure and damped domestic spending and investment. Tourist arrival numbers have plummeted. Exports have picked up recently but not by enough to counterbalance the fall in domestic demand which accounts for 80% of economic activity.

The central bank likely has decided rate cuts are a "blunt tool" that won't help boost growth while the political fighting continues, ANZ Bank said in a note to clients. Household debt also remains high, which makes it difficult for the bank to ease monetary policy too much, ANZ said.

Capital Economics is betting rates will remain on hold for the rest of 2014.

The central bank will want to keep rates high enough to ensure foreign capital doesn't flow out of the country, the London-based research firm said.

Some analysts say the deteriorating situation could force more easing. Su Sian Lim, an economist at HSBC, said the bank could possibly cut rates by 0.25 percentage point at its June meeting.

But Paiboon Kittisrikangwan, an assistant governor at the bank, said Wednesday that rate cuts won't help Thailand's economy.

"It should be noted that the restriction to the local economic recovery, at present, barely has much to do with the issues of liquidity and financial costs," he said.

"Our survey has found that the number one issue among entrepreneurs is political uncertainties in the country."

Thailand's deputy prime minister, Niwattumrong Boonsongpaisan, said Monday the government predicts growth of between 1% and 3% in 2014. "If the political unrest drags on until the end of the year, Thailand's GDP may contract or register no growth," he added.

The country will announce first-quarter gross domestic product data on May 19. The bank's next monetary policy meeting is in mid-June. "The political situation does not appear to be improving, and we expect this will take a significant chunk out of first quarter GDP growth," said Fred Gibson at Moody'sAnalytics.

Short sellers have slightly increased their bets against insurance claims processor Quindell, wagering that its shares are still overvalued even after hundreds of millions of pounds were wiped from its market value.

Quindell, which has promised to "revolutionise the insurance industry", is the subject of a scathing dossier of allegations from a US investor, which questions the company's profitability and acquisitions strategy.

Its shares closed up 12.6 per cent yesterday at 26.65p, but remain nearly one-third below their level before the dossier was published.

Short sellers, however, do not appear to have taken advantage of Tuesday's share price fall to exit their positions at a profit.

The proportion of the company's shares that is on loan to short sellers rose from 6.2 per cent last week to 6.3 per cent on Tuesday, according to financial data provider Markit.

The rise "suggests short sellers believe holding on to their positions is still likely to still offer them results", said Karl Loomes, a market analyst at Astec Analytics.

Quindell was one of the best-performing stocks on London's junior market AIM following a flurry of acquisitions and contract wins which it said were worth over 1bn in revenue.

It signed a significant deal this month with RAC, handing Quindell the right to handle the car insurer's telematics customers.

Quindell had been targeting a move to a full London listing in May, with inclusion in the FTSE 250 share index by June. A prospectus for its London listing was due to be published today, although it is unclear this will go ahead.

In its dossier Gotham argued that Quindell's shares are worth 3p each. That contrasts with a price target of 87p set by analysts from Canaccord, a broker to Quindell.

Before this week, Quindell and its founder Rob Terry were dismissive of the prospects of short sellers criticising their business.

"[T]o the company's knowledge no one has made any significant money out of shorting Quindell's stock, whereas private individuals and institutions have made significant money from investing in it," it said in a statement to the FT last week.

Quindell's largest shareholders include fund managers M&G, Investec and Fidelity, which together own over 16 per cent of the business.

FT REPORT - WORKING IN INTERNATIONAL BANKING
IT staff move to centre stage as customers take to their tablets

By Virginia Marsh 
851 words
24 April 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys REC1
07
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology Changing habits are boosting demand for digital leaders and specialists, says Virginia Marsh

Any doubt that IT drives today's banks has just been dispelled by Michael Lewis's book Flash Boys . Delving deep into the world of high-frequency trading, the American writer describes the astonishing lengths to which US investment banks go to gain milliseconds over their rivals.

But while their traders compete with upstarts that go as far as boring through mountains to lay hundreds of miles of fibre-optic cable between US exchanges, banks' customers are demanding new technology of a less controversial kind.

Many already expect to perform their mainstream banking in seconds using smartphones and tablets, a way of life that was a novelty five years ago.

Francesco Gonzlez, chief executive of Spain's BBVA, a digital pioneer, believes that within three years only 5 per cent of consumer interaction will be through physical banks. In the US, Bank of America customers already pay in 9 per cent of their cheques by emailing a digital photo.

As banks scramble to keep up, information technology and the people who make it work have moved centre stage.

"IT has gone from being a service to being a core part of how a bank faces its market," says Mike Beresford, managing director of specialist recruiter Randstad Technologies in the UK.

"Previously, a [chief information officer] would have input into board discussions. Now, CIOs work very much arm in arm with the [chief operating officer] or chief executive. They have become integral."

Moreover, there is a bigger C-suite to fill: enter the CDO, which can stand for both chief digital and chief data officer.

The former - described by Wired, the technology magazine, as the new "it" position for fast-growing businesses - oversees digitisation and new digital technologies, while the latter is responsible for the governance and use of an organisation's information, or data.

How banks manage "big data" - the vast amounts of information generated by online activity and storable relatively inexpensively - is critical. But it is something they are only just waking up to.

"Banks can use this data to forge ahead," says Kimberly Kelly Fahey, at Randstad in New York. "But they need real people to analyse it and make it usable."

As such, demand for specialists in data mining, analysis, management and storage is "beyond belief", says a senior financial services executive. Other hot areas include: message-based services architecture and design skills; cyber security; and good "change team" and project managers.

Creating chief digital and chief data officers is far from straightforward and banks are struggling with their organisational structures. For example, with web design being intrinsic to business models, should those working on it be classed as IT or marketing staff?

"Banks are in transition," says Rob Toguri, enterprise intelligence partner for the Emea region at EY, the professional services firm. "How are they doing technology and where does it sit in the organisation? No longer are technical staff confined to technical discussions. They need to know the vocabulary of business first."

To that end, banks are pulling in staff from sectors such as retail and media.

"The biggest thing I am looking for, in one word, is diversity, not just nationality, but diverse sets of opinions and experiences, and ways of working," says Aman Narain, global head of digital banking at Standard Chartered, the emerging markets specialist (see article, right) .

In the US, banks underinvested in IT talent after the 2008 financial crisis and staff sat tight. Now many, otherwise satisfied with their jobs, are prepared to move for better pay - and are finding offers, says Ms Fahey.

There is movement in the UK too, says Mr Beresford, but the bigger trend is for counter-offers. His team saw more of them in the first quarter than in the whole of last year. For Mr Narain, who is based in Singapore, attrition comes from staff forming start-ups.

Not surprisingly, reliance on IT contractors, long a feature of banking, is on the increase. In UK investment banking they constitute as much as 40 per cent of IT staff, says Stephen Rutherford, managing director at Michael Page, the recruiter. This not only reflects market dynamics, it is structural, he says, noting that contracting is becoming a career choice rather than something done between jobs or pre-retirement.

Nearshoring, often to southern and eastern Europe, is also on the rise. Deutsche Bank, for example, last year opened a global technology centre in Bucharest, the Romanian capital.

The cost difference with India has narrowed. However, recruiters say banks in Europe also want higher value functions and processes near at hand.

This reflects pressure for faster turnround times and a belief that local teams have the educational edge and language skills both to create the richest new content and apps, and to cope with the new, tougher regulation and compliance demands that prompted the need for more robust IT departments in the first place.

Ratings Firms Ride Bond Resurgence

By Timothy W. Martin 
1,070 words
24 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

Six years after getting a failing grade for their role in the financial crisis, credit-rating firms are at the top of the class.

Riding a global bond boom, the two biggest U.S. firms, Standard & Poor's Ratings Services and Moody's Investors Service, this month are expected to post record first-quarter profits. Fitch Ratings said in its annual filing this month that 2013 was "one of its best years ever."

Beyond the spike in bond deals, the resurgence is due largely to the absence of any major changes to the industry since the crisis: The business model, in which debt issuers pay for ratings, remains in place; regulations proposed years ago are yet to be implemented; and new competitors have gotten little more than a toehold.

This is despite heated rhetoric from regulators and legislators in the wake of the crisis, in which they castigated the firms for giving elevated ratings to mortgage-related bonds that later soured. The industry's ability to escape relatively unscathed is in contrast to others, most notably big Wall Street banks, that have been the target of a wave of regulation designed to change behavior that contributed to the meltdown.

"There was a lot of talk, but there wasn't a lot of action," said Marc Joffe, a former senior director at Moody's and now principal consultant at Public Sector Credit Solutions in Walnut Creek, Calif., who has been critical over the lack of major changes to the ratings world.

The issuer-pay model has been lucrative for the ratings firms, as guidelines for many institutional investors still require bonds to be rated, often dictating that it be done by the larger firms.

The firms say the recent surge in profits ratifies efforts to patch up internal trouble spots after the crisis and highlights their success in selling clients even more analysis and cutting certain costs.

Some litigation remains pending, including a $5 billion lawsuit by the U.S. government against S&P, but executives say the storm clouds over the industry largely have passed.

"By virtually every measure that we have, we see that investors are coming to us more. There is a thirst for more information," said Mark Oline, global head of business and relationship management at Fitch Ratings.

Stock investors have taken note. Over the past year, shares of the parent companies of the two big firms, Moody's Corp. and McGraw Hill Financial Inc., have outpaced the broader market by more than two times. Fitch Ratings is owned by Fimalac SA and Hearst Corp.

The three big firms, which held 95% of market share before the crisis, have roughly the same amount today. Moody's reports earnings on Friday, and McGraw Hill reports on Tuesday.

Credit-rating companies measure an issuer's ability to repay debt, assigning alphanumeric grades, like "Aa1" for safer entities or "C" for riskier ones. The firms are paid by the issuers, on the grounds that the ratings serve as a guide for investors, and their grades accompany nearly all bond deals.

The global surge in bond issuance, prompted by low interest rates set by central banks, has been the main driver of the firms' profits, analysts say. The U.S. corporate-bond market hit a record $1.48 trillion in borrowing last year, according to data provider Dealogic. The overall debt markets hit a combined $12.7 trillion in 2012 and 2013, the second highest two-year stretch ever, Dealogic said.

But the firms also have expanded further into other products besides basic ratings. Most notably, they are selling their proprietary analysis, the research and market data that shape ratings, for additional fees. These new analytics businesses are highly profitable because they mostly bundle research that has been compiled already.

Moody'sAnalytics has nearly doubled in size since the unit was formed by combining several separate business lines in 2007, bringing in $913 million in revenue last year. It now represents 31% of Moody's Corp.'s overall revenue.

Revenue at S&P's subscription-based analytics division was $1.1 billion in 2013, up 27% in the past four years. Revenue at Fitch Solutions, which entices customers with access to 35 years of historical ratings, has increased 69% during the same stretch.

S&P, meanwhile, also cut labor costs by sending data-heavy work to operations in India.

S&P employees each produce about $679,000 of revenue a year on average, the company said at an investor day this year. That outpaces every Wall Street investment bank except Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley, according to FactSet. The average information-technology firm produces about $467,000 per employee. The other ratings firms declined to provide information on revenue per employee.

The surge in profits, and the industry's comeback in general, raises the hackles of critics who feel the rating firms were never held to account for their role in the financial crisis.

Following the housing-market collapse, ratings firms saw revenue bottom out in 2008. Fitch dropped 21%, Moody's sunk 32% and S&P declined 49%. Critics zeroed on the conflicts inherent in the issuer-pays business model, noting that it provided incentives for firms to give rosy ratings to deals to win business.

The ratings firms say less reliance by investors on ratings is healthy and lead to lower risks, as they tend to perform more due diligence. They also point to internal adjustments they have made in recent years. "People underestimate the amount of change that the business has gone through," said Moody's Corp. Chief Executive Raymond McDaniel. He said Moody's has established more internal controls separating its research analysts from its sales team. Likewise, S&P says it has spent $400 million on compliance and risk-management tools since 2008.

Douglas Peterson, McGraw Hill's chief executive, who previously led S&P Ratings, also noted that the economic environment is different than it was in the years before the crisis.

Most of the growth in bond issuance is in corporate and noninvestment-grade debt, which is relatively well understood by investors as compared with mortgage-backed securities that were in vogue leading up to the crisis. "That's a very different story," Mr. Peterson said.

The former head of Citigroup Inc.'s mortgage unit has joined First Data Corp., the credit card processor owned by KKR & Co., according to an internal memo reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

Sanjiv Das, who served as chief executive of CitiMortgage from 2008 to 2013, has taken the role as First Data's chief of staff and is joining the company's management committee, according to the internal memo sent Thursday.

Mr. Das is the latest Wall Street recruit to join First Data. He was hired by Frank Bisignano, the former co-chief operating officer of J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., who joined First Data last April as chief executive. Mr. Bisignano has been charged with making the company profitable to prepare it for a possible initial public offering.

Mr. Bisignano has recruited so many former colleagues that First Data agreed in January to pay millions of dollars to J.P. Morgan in exchange for a promise that the bank wouldn't contest new hires, said people familiar with the agreement.

Former J.P. Morgan executives that have joined First Data include Cindy Armine, the bank's former chief compliance officer, and Heidi Miller, the former president of J.P. Morgan International. Ms. Armine, who left J.P. Morgan in February, serves as First Data's chief control officer.

First Data has struggled with a heavy debt load since KKR took it private in 2007 for roughly $26 billion in one of the biggest leveraged buyouts of all time. The company, which processes thousands of credit and debit card transactions, has lost money every year since the deal was completed as consumers slashed spending during the financial crisis.

Mr. Bisignano has moved quickly to cut costs. He replaced 401 (k) contributions to employees and cash bonuses with stock grants. The moves took effect Jan. 1 and are expected to save the company $60 million in cash this year.

He has also been trying to grow the company beyond a processor to a provider of services like data analytics to merchants. First Data recently launched Clover Station, a point-of-sale system for small and medium-size businesses.

Mr. Das, a former Morgan Stanley and American Express executive, spent five years beginning in 2008 trying to clean up Citigroup's mortgage business. He was replaced in 2013 by Jane Fraser.

In the memo, Mr. Bisignano said that Mr. Das would look at opportunities for the company and become more familiar with its international businesses.

Hedge Funds Don't Bud This Spring

By Rob Copeland 
770 words
18 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

A tough winter is turning into an ugly spring for top hedge funds, as a tendency to cluster into the same trades is stinging many star managers.

A number of multibillion-dollar funds are down sharply this month, pushing losses into double digits on a percentage basis for the year at some firms, according to investor communications reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

Some of the common losing positions in recent weeks were in technology and biotechnology shares, including Google Inc. and Valeant Pharmaceuticals International Inc.

The downturn at many funds, including Viking Global Investors LP and JAT Capital Management LP, surpasses the decline in the broader market, where the S&P 500 index was down 1.2%, including dividends, for the year through the end of last week. That is the same period covered in the latest investor communications.

Entering the year, many fund managers were betting that last year's bull-market rally would continue, and they boosted their exposure to certain top-performing stocks.

"The big momentum trade has come off. It's a bit of a no-man's-land out there," said Jon Yalmokas, head of the Americas prime brokerage for Bank of AmericaMerrill Lynch. He noted he hasn't seen evidence of hedge funds capitulating on their positions, or rotating into betting against more stocks.

For the first three months of the year, hedge funds across the industry gained on average 1.1%, the lowest since 2009, according to research firm HFR Inc. The slide accelerated for some firms this month, especially those focused on stocks. The median equity-focused hedge fund lost more than 2% in the first two weeks of April, according to Morgan Stanley.

Viking, a $28 billion Greenwich, Conn., firm led by amateur triathlete Andreas Halvorsen, lost 4.2% in March -- its second-worst month in a decade, according to investors -- and has dropped almost an additional 4% in the first two weeks of April. The bulk of the losses were in technology and health-care shares, investors said.

John Thaler's JAT saw a similar trajectory. The Greenwich firm dropped 9.3% in March and has slid more than 4% this month, leaving it down about 10% for the year, people familiar with the firm said. A spokesman for the $2.2 billion firm declined to comment.

The turmoil comes amid a spate of market volatility that theoretically should benefit funds that can place bets both on and against stocks. But analysts said many firms piled into the same names -- with both long and short bets -- and thus saw their fortunes fall together.

"Among hedge-fund managers, rampant duplication has become common. Everything is so saturated. As soon as anyone gets an idea, everyone else jumps on board," said Brian Shapiro, founder of hedge-fund analytics company Simplify LLC.

Some funds bucked the trend. The $1.7 billion Horseman Capital Management Ltd.'s Global fund rose almost 10% in the first quarter, fueled by contrary positions betting against stocks in sectors such as oil exploration and real estate, according to an investor update. The London-based firm wasn't immediately available for comment.

But recent losses were more common. Even so-called macro funds, which can invest globally and across various asset classes including stocks, bonds and currencies, have been hit hard.

Discovery Global Macro Fund Ltd., part of Robert Citrone's $15 billion South Norwalk, Conn., firm, has slid 6% this month and is down more than 14% on the year, according to an investor document. The fund has been felled by bearish bets on emerging markets and wagers that technology stocks would rise.

In a previous investor communication, Mr. Citrone described the start of the year as a "perfect storm" of negative events for the firm.

Many funds are standing their ground. Mr. Halvorsen of Viking told investors in a letter this month the firm had actually increased its exposure to rising stocks.

The principals of Contour Asset Management, a $1.7 billion hedge fund part-owned by Swedish alternatives firm Brummer & Partners, wrote to investors that the New York firm had reduced leverage by about 20% amid the "frustrating" decline in technology, media and telecommunications equities.

Founders David Meyer and Seth Wunder, who were sanguine that the pullback may have helped in "removing the froth and some of the excessive optimism," added they are not yet betting on a long-term sector-wide decline.

"Calling a price bubble is not an easy feat," they wrote.

Libyan wealth fund to appoint investment managers

By Cynthia O'Murchu 
818 words
17 April 2014
11:24
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The Libyan Investment Authority plans to outsource its investment management, expand in London and implement international standards of governance, as it seeks to move beyond allegations of past mismanagement, cronyism and failed investments.

"LIA will no longer manage investments as it did in the past - we will manage external managers", Abdulmagid Breish, the chairman of the Libyan Investment Authority, told the Financial Times in an interview today.

As part of its restructuring plans, the LIA wants to expand its operations in London, where is already owns a valuable commercial property portfolio. "[London] will be a centre of excellence where a lot of the analytics, a lot of the research and the management of external managers will be handled," Mr Breish said.

"We will have to prepare our risk profile and allocation," he added. "Once that is done, we will identify which best in class fund managers will be suitable".

Mr Breish, a former banker at Arab Banking Corporation, took over the reins of the ailing Libyan sovereign wealth fund last year and continued previous efforts to restructure what was previously one of the world's most opaque state investment vehicles.

After the fall of the Gaddafi regime nearly three years ago, the LIA began to take stock of its portfolio of holdings. According to a recent valuation by Deloitte cited by Mr Breish, the LIA is now worth $66bn. It still owns some 550 companies and direct investments in north Africa, the Middle East and Europe - some of which it intends to wind up.

Most of LIA's assets remain frozen. Nonetheless, Mr Breish says, the LIA is already being courted by international financial organisations looking to pitch their business.

"We've had many different financial institutions pitching for the past three months," he said. "We've been telling them we're not ready, we're building restructuring and enhancing our capabilities and when we're ready we can engage."

The LIA also plans to establish a "future generations fund", to be replenished through oil income, and a "budget stabilisation fund", from which the Libyan government could borrow if it has a budget deficit.

This overhaul of investment strategy and governance is being carried in tandem with a quest to recover some of the massive losses the LIA suffered on investments with some of the world's biggest banks, before the Libyan revolution.

After its inception in 2006, the LIA sought investment opportunities for Libya's oil wealth. By 2010, it had built a cash-heavy portfolio, which also featured equity stakes in companies such as Italy's UniCredit bank and Pearson, owner of the Financial Times, in which it still has a 2.98 per cent shareholding.

However, the fund also entered into a series of risky derivative transactions which led to billion-dollar losses, some of which the LIA is now looking to recover through the courts. In January this year, the LIA sued Goldman Sachs over derivative trades in excess of $1bn. In March it filed a $1.5bn claim against Socit Gnrale accusing the French bank of helping to funnel bribes worth tens of millions of dollars to close associates of Saif al-Islam, the son of former Libyan leader Muammer Gaddafi.

Both banks deny the claims made against them, and Goldman Sachs has applied for summary judgment on its case.

In addition, the LIA is considering taking action to recover $700m it invested in Netherlands-based Palladyne International Asset Management and to investigate several other smaller transactions.

Palladyne International Asset management, which is led by Ismael Abudher, the son-in-law of Libya's former oil minister, is accused in a separate lawsuit filed by a previous employee in the United Staes of serving as a "kickback and money laundering operation" for the former Libyan regime.

A spokesperson for Palladyne International Asset Management said:

"Palladyne has always conducted its business to the highest of standards. Its chief executive, Ismael Abudher, has never been part of any Libyan regime, and he and the company have only ever acted in the best interests of their clients."

Steering the LIA in post-Gaddafi years has not been plain sailing. "We were starting form zero" Mr Breish said. " The LIA was an institution that's been left to rot".

Mr Breish's predecessor, Mohsen Derregia, was ousted in February last year, and blamed interference from well-connected individuals and entrenched interests for his dismissal.

"I am not inhibited by pressure by any party," Mr Breish said. "This does not mean that there isn't pressure. You see this coming from certain quarters who are anxious about their future or are anxious about uncovering certain things that may pertain to them."

However, he remains positive for the future of the LIA. "I will put into LIA whatever is best practice and what international sovereign wealth funds do."

COMPANIES
Fintech start-ups chip away at banks; News analysis

By Sally Davies in London 
1,440 words
14 April 2014
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

High street lenders strike back by launching their own digital products, writes Sally Davies

The tipping point for Jozef Wallis was when he discovered he was paying nearly 500 a month to transfer the salaries of his small team of software developers from London to Slovenia.

"Traditional banks in the UK aren't good at managing international accounts for companies of our size," says the entrepreneur, 34, whose start-up, Toothpick, helps users find and book dentist appointments online.

So for the past year, Mr Wallis has been using TransferWise - a three-year-old London-based app and website that allows people and small businesses to transfer money between international accounts more cheaply than they can with banks.

More than just a disgruntled customer, Mr Wallis typifies the growth of new technology start-ups in London that threaten to shake up financial services in the UK and beyond. As well as money transfer , innovation in the consumer-facing so-called fintech sector is happening in asset management, mobile payments and crowdfunding.

"There is no better place in the world to set up a fintech firm than in London," says Ismail Ahmed, the Somali co-founder of WorldRemit, a money transfer service that raised $40m from venture capitalists last month. "As the global centre of financial services and a tech hub , London gives fintech entrepreneurs access to an unparalleled pool of talent, from developers to product managers to compliance officers to sales."

Financial technology start-ups in the UK raised $700m from investors between 2008 and 2013, according to research from Accenture. While that is less than the funding raised by Silicon Valley fintech companies over the same period, the growth in funding for UK companies during that time was much faster. As with disruptive start-ups in industries ranging from air travel to retail, fintech entrepreneurs use their lower costs to undercut established companies.

"The incumbents make so much money and with such an easy life, why would they change?" says Nick Hungerford. The former wealth manager at Barclays is now chief executive of Nutmeg, a two-year-old start-up that allows consumers to set up online investment portfolios. Nutmeg has about 25,000 users with portfolios ranging from 1,000 to millions of pounds.

The company charges a 1 per cent fee, which drops to 0.3 per cent for portfolios valued at more than 500,000. Partly because it communicates with clients online rather than by phone or in person, the service is about four to five times cheaper than traditional asset managers - who also tend to deal with only wealthy people, Mr Hungerford says.

The sea change promised by fintech start-ups extends far beyond a race to the bottom on price. The proliferation of smartphones has made consumers demand more convenient and intuitive ways of purchasing financial services products, while the data they generate could reveal valuable insights about how to improve products.

In 2013, 12.4m people in the UK downloaded banking apps, and conducted 18.6m transactions a week on their mobile phones - more than double the rate for the previous year, according to the British Banking Association.

Anthony Browne, the BBA's chief executive, says: "The seismic decline in the use of banks' high street outlets is largely explained by a quiet, but dramatic, revolution in the way we spend, move and manage our money."

But for all its promise to improve the lives of consumers, fintech is still small. FundingCircle, a four-year-old online platform that allows people to lend directly to small businesses, has processed just 240m of loans, compared with the 38bn that UK banks lent to small businesses in the past year alone.

Several obstacles stand in the way of fintech growing big enough to seriously challenge the likes of high street banks such as HSBC, NatWest and Barclays.

Regulation is one hurdle. "The payments industry is highly regulated and has an intense focus on security at every step of the often-convoluted payments processing network," says Richard Moulds, head of product strategy at Thales e-Security, a company that sells software to protect data relating to digital payments. "It can be tricky to get new technologies approved."

Big banks are also striking back by launching their own digital and mobile services.

Barclays launched Pingit two years ago - a smartphone app that allows customers from any UK bank send money to one another using only a telephone number.

The bank says that the app is responsible for attracting between 20 per cent and 30 per cent of its new corporate customers.

Similar initiatives include Paym , a service run by the Payments Council, the industry body responsible for UK payment mechanisms. The service lets consumers send money by punching in a mobile telephone number. Another example is Zapp, a mobile payments service controlled by a consortium of high street banks that is due to be introduced this year.

Established companies are also co-opting promising start-ups. London now has four corporate-sponsored fintech accelerators - intensive courses for start-ups that usually involve exchanging the start-ups' equity for an injection of cash, as well as free office space and mentorship about how to grow.

The latest of these accelerators is Barclays', announced last month in Mile End in a building once known as "The Harrods of the east".

Barclays will give the companies accepted to its accelerator access to the software code for Pingit, so that the start-ups can build smartphone apps and programs to integrate with the payments service. "What started as a product is evolving into a platform," says Derek White, chief design officer at Barclays.

Meanwhile, MasterCard, Rabobank and Lloyds Banking Group have put their weight behind Startup Bootcamp, a global accelerator group that is launching a specialist fintech initiative near Tower Bridge in London. They will provide cash as well as mentorship, data and access to their systems' software code.

Traditional financial services groups argue that their scale, experience and brand - as well as the resources they can bring to bear - make them better placed than lone start-ups to offer consumers the choice they crave.

But some observers are sceptical that the incumbents, facing increased capital constraints and regulation, will be willing to cannibalise their own businesses.

"Innovators are nibbling away at all the different financial services," says Mr Hungerford. "One day soon the big banks will turn round and realise that half their sector has been taken out."

What London's key start ups are hoping to revolutionies

International money transfer

WorldRemit, Moni Technologies, Azimo, TransferWise

What the problems are: Wire services and banks often charge high fees to send and receive money, take a long time to process it, and are not always clear about the forex rates they are charging customers. Going into a branch is inconvenient, and the agents in corner shops do not always do reliable identity checks.

What fintech is doing: Using mobile technology and social networks to make money transfers cheaper, quicker, and more convenient and secure. Azimo integrates with Facebook, for example, to verify users' identity.

Payments

Pingit, Photopay, Zapp, Paym

What the problems are: Sending money via online banking to another person or paying bills requires finding out and typing in lots of numbers.

What fintech is doing: Finding ways to speed up payment by cutting out the numbers. Photopay - a start-up from Croatia that is part of Accenture's FinTech Innovation Lab in London - is an app that lets users take a photo of a bill and make a payment with one tap using their mobile banking app.

Personal finance and asset management

Nutmeg, Rplan, Blue Speck Financials

What the problems are: Private wealth managers have high minimum thresholds, opaque fees structures and confusing jargon.

What fintech is doing: Start-ups are helping different kinds of users manage their investments via data visualisation, analytics and mobile apps.

Nutmeg offers users a choice of investing around particular goals, such a child's university fees. Rplan lets you plug in your existing assets and then provides you with a risk profile as well as plan new investments.

Crowdfunding

FundingCircle, Seedrs, Zopa

What the problems are: Small companies are starved for credit from banks and traditional lenders, and regular people who want to make money by lending to individuals and small businesses have not had a way to do so.

What fintech is doing: Start-ups offer a software platform - essentially a market for loans and equity - for people to make money by financing small businesses and individuals.

Bolton: 'I was wrong about the market in China'; Fund focus

By Bradley Gerrard

English
Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Gearing was main problem for China Special Situations trust, writes Bradley Gerrard

Star manager Anthony Bolton has admitted he was wrong about the Chinese stock market , as he hung up his fund management hat after 35 years.

Mr Bolton, who is best known for his stellar performance while managing Fidelity's flagship Special Situations fund for 28 years, will now become a member of the board but will no longer run any money.

He came out of retirement in 2010 following a trip to China, and the launch of his Fidelity China Special Situations investment trust was a feeding frenzy for his hoards of faithful investors.

After an initial surge the Chinese market slumped and Mr Bolton's focus on smaller companies and his use of gearing - borrowing money to boost the trust's pool of investments - magnified his investors' losses.

In early 2012 Mr Bolton extended previous plans for a three-year tenure to try to turn the trust's portfolio around. It has now beaten the wider Chinese stock market, but it is still trading only slightly above its launch value.

"The most disappointing thing for me - and I am happy to admit it - is that I was wrong about the market in China," the manager said during a press briefing on his last day in the office on March 31.

He points to the trust's borrowing as a main detractor of performance.

"I had a geared fund when everything was going down. I made a big play to 'new China' and in the past 18 months that has really come into its own. But I would have liked to have seen a better share price at the end of my tenure," he says.

In share price total return terms the trust gained 5.2 per cent from launch to the end of March, versus a 4 per cent loss on the MSCI China index, according to FE Analytics, the data provider. A year ago it lagged behind the index with an overall loss of 4 per cent. Management of the trust has now passed to Dale Nicholls.

One of the trust's heavy exposures is in technology, a sector in which Mr Bolton made key calls during his tenure on the Special Situations fund.

His decision to back Nokia, the mobile phone maker, in 1993 and to buy shares in security company Securicor in the belief that its stake in Cellnet, the mobile subsidiary it co-owned with BT, would push its value up proved to be major moves.

While the tech stocks in his China trust may not all have paid off in the same way, his predictions about technology have been proved right overall.

Mr Bolton is pessimistic about economic growth prospects in China, predicting 6 per cent a year as a likely figure - lower than the consensus 7.5 per cent.

He welcomes the current Chinese government, headed by premier Li Keqiang, saying it has made significant attempts to enact positive economic change and eradicate corruption.

Mr Bolton says Chinese stock market valuations are "very low against history" and as far as two standard deviations below their long-term level.

Bradley Gerrard is news editor at Investment Adviser

U.S. Banks Facing New Capital Curb

By Ryan Tracy and Stephanie Armour 
814 words
8 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.) 

WASHINGTON -- U.S. regulators are set to impose another curb on risk taking at the largest U.S. banks Tuesday as part of a continuing push to force big banks to gird themselves against periods of market stress.

Under the new "leverage ratio," scheduled for a vote by the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. and the Federal Reserve, the eight biggest U.S. firms would have to double the amount of capital they hold as protection against every loan, investment, building, security and other asset on their books -- not just the risky ones.

The rule could force big banks to add tens of billions of dollars in new capital, though many have been bulking up since regulators first floated a leverage ratio in July. It would require the biggest companies to maintain loss-absorbing capital worth at least 5% of their assets, and their FDIC-insured bank subsidiaries would have to keep a minimum leverage ratio of 6%. That compares with the 3% set by international accord.

For the largest banks, satisfying the new requirement will likely be manageable in the near term, but analysts warned it could constrain future growth since it would limit each bank's ability to increase its asset base, forcing it to either raise more cash from investors or shed assets elsewhere if it begins to bump up against the ratio's limits.

"In an environment where you are getting strong economic growth, it could be a limiting factor with how much you can grow," said Brian Kleinhanzl, an analyst at Keefe, Bruyette, & Woods.

KBW, in a report issued after regulators proposed the new limits in July, estimated the eight banks faced a net collective shortfall of about $50.2 billion to meet the 6% threshold, with J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Bank of New York Mellon Corp. facing the widest gaps as a percentage of their existing capital. Many banks have been building up capital since that July analysis, Mr. Kleinhanzl said.

Tuesday's regulatory move is just one of several pending rules targeted at banking behemoths like Bank of America Corp. and Wells Fargo & Co. Regulators also are expected to impose an additional risk-based capital charge for banks considered central to financial stability and perhaps another capital penalty for banks that are involved in potentially volatile markets for short-term borrowing and lending.

In addition to the capital rules, regulators also are implementing the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial law, which included a number of curbs on banks, such as the Volcker rule's ban on speculative investing.

"We're just accepting a public policy of really stringently regulating banks, especially the biggest ones, and then letting the chips fall where they may," said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the advisory firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc.

When it was proposed last year, the leverage ratio sent a shock wave through the banking industry, which hadn't expected U.S. regulators to take such an aggressive tack.

Now, after months of continued debate about whether large banks remain "too big to fail" come the next financial crisis, the same rules are "remarkably, and in seemingly short order, becoming noncontroversial," Ms. Petrou said.

Banks have balked at the increased minimum ratio since it was proposed last year, saying it will curtail lending and saddle them with more costs that leave them at a competitive disadvantage. But of late, much of their attention in Washington has been focused on fighting even more radical proposals from Capitol Hill, such as a hefty new tax on their assets or a 15% minimum capital requirement.

Still, the exact impact remains to be seen. J.P. Morgan and Bank of New York, for instance, have brokerage businesses that involve holding large books of assets that could count against them in a leverage ratio, depending on the specifics of how it is calculated.

Bank of New York had no comment Monday but has previously said the regulators' proposal is "unwise" and would "totally change the fundamental balance" of the current U.S. capital rules. A J.P. Morgan spokesman didn't respond to a request for comment.

While regulators will finalize the overarching 5% and 6% requirements, they also will propose tweaks in the way certain types of assets are tallied for calculating the denominator of the ratio, according to people familiar with the matter. Those tweaks will hew closely to revisions agreed to by international regulators on the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision in January, these people said.

In general, the new parameters for counting derivatives and other assets are expected to make the leverage ratio tougher on banks than the original U.S. proposal, though they are less stringent than an earlier version published by the Basel Committee.
